Testibus his: Brocein agus Cormac ab Turbruaid agus Morgann mac Dhonnchaidh agus Gille-Pheadair mac Dhonnchaidh agus Malaechin agus dà mhac Matni agus maithean Bhuchan uile ’n a fhianais an Heláin.

<eng>VII.

RUBRIC.

Here thou givest Host to him.

VIII.

THE COLOPHON.

(Be it) on the conscience of every one with whom shall be the booklet with beauty that he bestow a blessing on the soul of the poor wretch who has copied it.

[TD 192]

IX.

CHARTER OF KING DAVID I (1124-1153).

Dauid . rex Scottorum omnibus probis hominibus suis . salutes. Sciatis quod clerici . de Dér . sunt quieti et immunes ab omni laicorum officio . et exactione indebita sicut in libro eorum scriptum est . et dirationauerunt apud Bánb . et iurauerunt apud Abberdeon . quapropter firmiter precipio . ut nullus eis . aut eorum catellis . aliquam iniuriam inferre presumat. Teste Gregorio episcopo . de Duncallden. Teste Andrea episcopo . de Catness. Teste Samsone episcopo . de Brechin. Teste Doncado comite . de Fíb . et Malmori . d’Athótla . et Ggillebrite comite . d’Engus. et Ghgillcomded . mac Aed . et Brocin . et Cormac . de Turbrud . et Adam . mac . Ferdomnac . et Gillendrias mac . Mátni . apud . Abberdeon.

[TD 193]

NOTES.

Page 1.

<gai>taobh an iar:<eng> the western side. The primary meaning of iar is “after”; air (O.Ir. ar; Gaulish are) means originally “before,” “in front of.” In regard to the points of the compass, the observer is supposed to face the rising sun. Thus the east is in front of him, the west behind him, and the south to his right; hence <gai>an ear<eng> (O.Ir. an-air), <gai>an iar, a deas<eng> respectively; tuath originally meant “left,” hence “north.” In O.Ir. the terminology is fuller:—

<gai>t-air,<eng> in the east.
<gai>s-air,<eng> eastwards.
<gai>an-air,<eng> from the east.

<gai>t-iar,<eng> in the west.
<gai>s-iar,<eng> westwards.
<gai>an-iar,<eng> from the west.

See further Thurneysen, Handbook of Old Irish, p. 287.

<gai>A’ Chuideachd Urramach …<eng> the Honourable Society for Propagating Christian Knowledge in the Highlands and Islands of Scotland was founded in 1709. It is now merged in the Highland Trust.

<gai>cuideachd a bhreac:<eng> here a represents the particle do, which in Middle Irish came to be used commonly to introduce a relative clause. Note: (1) a = do cannot be used to denote the relation of genitive or dative, but only that of subject or object. (2) It introduces only positive relative clauses. (3) It aspirates the following verb whenever the latter admits of aspiration. Cf. <gai>a chuireadh air bonn; a chuir.

a réir:<eng> formerly <gai>do réir,<eng> “(according) to will”; dat. of <gai>rìar,<eng> fem.

[TD 194

<gai>tighean a thogail:<eng> formerly <gai>do thogail,<eng> “to lifting”; so, <gai>croiteag a chur air leth.<eng> Note that the formula undergoes no change, whatever the number or gender of the preceding noun.

<gai>air a cumail suas:<eng> “on her keeping up”; a, poss. pron. of 3 sg. fem. So, <gai>air a chumail<eng> (3 sg. mas.); <gai>air an cumail<eng> (3 pl. mas. and fem.), <gai>air mo chumail<eng> (1 sg. mas. and fem.), etc. Cf. <gai>gu robh an tigh air a thogail.<eng> In these expressions, <gai>air<eng> represents the old prep. <gai>iar,<eng> “after.” See MacBain, Et. Dict. s.v. air.

<gai>’s an t-sealladh: for anns …<eng> It is, as a rule, better to write words in full and to avoid contractions. In certain cases, however, the contracted form has become stereotyped, and cannot be avoided, e.g., <gai>do’n t-sùil; b’ann leis a’ chuideachd; f’a chomhair; d’a h-ùir.<eng> It will be observed that in the extracts from the older writers, beginning at p. 132, contractions are comparatively rare. The use of unnecessary contractions is to be avoided in Gaelic as in English.


Page 2.

<gai>air chor ’s:<eng> ’s here represents <gai>agus.

a’ dion an dachaidh:<eng> a’ for ag, which is always written in full before verbal nouns, etc., beginning with a vowel. Before consonants the contracted form a’ is usual but not invariable in modern Gaelic.

<gai>blàr fuilteach:<eng> the reference is to <gai>Cath nan Sguab,<eng> fought, according to tradition, on the beach to the north side of Dùn Gallan in Colonsay. See Celtic Review, IX., 209.


Page 3.

Feminine nouns usually show i in dative sing. before a final consonant, e.g., <gai>cas, cois; brù, broinn; grian, gréin.<eng> Those that have i already before a final consonant in the nominative singular (slender stems) have the dative the same as the nominative, e.g., <gai>sùil, obair, fuil.<eng> But
(1) fem. nouns ending in a vowel or -achd have nom. dat. and gen. sing. alike, e.g., <gai>coille, teanga, mallachd.<eng>

[TD 195]

(2) Broad stems (i.e., ending on a consonant preceded by a broad vowel) with -a in gen. have dat. and nom. alike, e.g., <gai>luch, pìob, earb<eng>—a very small class.
N.B.—In masculine nouns the dative is always the same as the nominative, e.g., <gai>taobh, loch, cù.<eng>


Page 4.

<gai>mo sheana mhaighistir:<eng> the final a of sheana is merely a “glide” to help in passing from n and m, and is better omitted. Similarly in the body of words, the consonants l, n, r develop a short dull vowel sound when in contact with a succeeding g, gh; b, bh; m, mh; e.g., balg, dealbh, dearg, muinghin, Donnchadh, Fearchar. This “parasitic” vowel is never written by good writers at any stage of the history of the language. Note that in this position the liquid (l, n, r) is (1) long, (2) trilled.

<gai>tha mi creidsinn:<eng> for <gai>ag creidsinn;<eng> so, <gai>tha mi meas<eng> for <gai>tha mi a(g) meas.<eng> The omission of a letter is indicated by an apostrophe (a’ for ag); and as a rule the apostrophe should be reserved for this purpose. In doubtful cases it is safest to write the word in full.

<gai>’s ann le urram:<eng> here ’s stands for is, 3 sg. of subst. verb. We have already seen it used for <gai>anns<eng> and <gai>agus;<eng> it is also used for as, the relative form of the subst. verb, and for is, “and.” This suggests the desirability of avoiding this particular contraction as far as possible.


Page 6.

<gai>Alasdair Mac Mhaighistir Alasdair:<eng> Alexander Macdonald, son of Reverend Alexander Macdonald, an Episcopal minister, who lived at Dalilea, Moidart, was the great poet of the middle period of the 18th century. The dates of his birth and death are not ascertained; circ. 1695-1770.

<gai>Dughall Bo-chanan:<eng> Dugald Buchanan, b. 1716 at Ardoch, Strathyre, Perthshire; d. 1768 at Kinloch-Rannoch. See Songs of Dugald Buchanan, ed. Rev. Donald Maclean.

<gai>am meangan . .<eng> from D. Buchanan’s An Geamhradh, p. 56.

<gai>sheinn e ’òrain:<eng> here the apostrophe indicates the omission of a, his.


Page 8.

<gai>Cuairtear nan Gleann,<eng> edited by Dr Norman Macleod, appeared in May, 1840. It reached its fortieth number, and ended about the time of the Disruption of 1843.

<gai>Fear Tathaich nam Beann<eng> began in 1848, and lived for two years; twenty-four numbers were published, and a twenty-fifth printed but not published. It was edited by Dr Clark of Kilmallie, Dr N. Macleod’s son-in-law.

An Gaidheal began in June, 1871; the last number (the seventy-second) appeared in December, 1877.

<gai>talamh:<eng> usually, as here, mas. in nominative sg. and fem. in genitive sg.

<gai>a ghnàth:<eng> formerly <gai>do ghnàth,<eng> “for custom.”


Page 9.

<gai>de ’n chruinne-ché:<eng> de means “of,” “belonging to,” “made of,” concerning”; Lat. de. It should not be aspirated except in pronominal compounds (dheth, dhibh, etc.). The preposition do means “to.” It is important to avoid confusion between de and do.

<gai>no:<eng> “or.”

<gai>na:<eng> “than.”


Page 10.

<gai>cuid d’ a shlighibh: d’ a<eng> for <gai>de a,<eng> “of his.”

<gai>ata r’am faicinn: ri am faicinn,<eng> lit. “at their seeing,” i.e., that may be seen. Similarly <gai>ri m’ fhaicinn, ri t’ fhaicinn, r’ a fhaicinn,<eng> etc.


Page 11.

<gai>r’ a chéile: céile,<eng> “fellow,” “comrade,” now mostly (1) spouse, <gai>céile pòsda; bean chéile, fear céile;<eng> (2) in phrase <gai>a chéile,<eng> “each other,” lit. “(each) his fellow.”

[TD 197]

<gai>cho mhaith:<eng> aspiration after cho is very rare and dialectic.

<gai>is e tha gluasad:<eng> ag is understood before gluasad. Note that gluasad, being a verbal noun, is followed by the genitive.

<gai>os a chionn:<eng> “above his head”; cionn is the old dative of <gai>ceann<eng> (O.Ir. ciunn). In the old language most mas. nouns had a dative distinct from the nominative.

<gai>d’ a ionnsaigh:<eng> lit. “to visiting it,” i.e., “towards it”; a, poss. pron. 3 per. mas., an “objective genitive,” object of ionnsaigh.

<gai>’sam bith: anns am bith,<eng> “in the world”; also <gai>air bith; bith is akin to beatha, beò.<eng>


Page 12.

<gai>aig a leithid a dh’ astar:<eng> lit. “at its like of a distance”; a, “its,” refers to astar; a dh’ astar is colloquial for <gai>de astar<eng> (see Scottish Celtic Review, p. 294).

<gai>le eagnuigheachd mhiorbhuilich:<eng> observe the adjective inflected in the dative fem.; with a mas. noun in the dative the adj. would not inflect.


Page 13.

<gai>air chor as:<eng> read, <gai>air chor agus.

air an ais:<eng> ais in the old language means “the back”; <gai>air ais,<eng> backwards; the appropriate possessive pronoun is inserted, e.g., <gai>air m’ais, air t’ais, air (a) ais; air ar n-ais; air bhur n-ais; air an ais.

a rìs:<eng> also <gai>a rithis, a rithisd;<eng> its construction closely resembles that of <gai>air ais;<eng> O.Ir. afrithisi, back again, from a, the possessive, and frithisse (frith and éisse), back-track. Originally the possessive varied according to the subject. In time the original force of the phrase ceased to be felt, and either afrithisi (his or its back-track), or doridisi (thy back-track) was used without reference to the subject. (See Strachan: Stories from the Tain, p. 49).

<gai>ma ta:<eng> read, <gai>matà.

a chionn:<eng> for <gai>do chionn;<eng> for <gai>cionn<eng> v. p. 11.

[TD 198]

<gai>air do’n ghealaich:<eng> air represents <gai>iar,<eng> after, as in <gai>bha e air a mharbhadh,<eng> lit. “he was after his killing.”

<gai>a thaobh nan àitean:<eng> for <gai>do thaobh,<eng> which occurs in p. 9.

<gai>an uair a bheachdaicheas: beachdaicheas<eng> is itself the relative form of the verb, and strictly requires no a (do) before it to express relativity. The a has been introduced by analogy. So, an Ti (a) charnas, p. 14.

<gai>as géire tuigse:<eng> “who is,” etc.; 3 sg. pr. ind., relative form.

<gai>cha mheasa chomas:<eng> supply a, “his,” before chomas.

<gai>ann am briathraibh:<eng> the “reduplicated” form of the preposition <gai>an, ann,<eng> is seldom, if ever, necessary. Here, for instance, am briathraibh would be equally adequate.

<gai>theirteadh:<eng> “it was wont to be said.”

<gai>ar leam:<eng> “methinks.” O.Ir. inda (da, ata) limm; Mid. Ir. indar, dar, atar limm; Mod. Ir. dar liom.

<gai>tha bhuil:<eng> lit. “there is its effect”; i.e., “the effect is seen”; supply a, “its.”

<gai>mar gu’m b’eadh:<eng> “as it were”; eadh, O.Ir. ed, the old 3 per. pron. sing. neut., survives only in certain phrases: <gai>is eadh, ’s eadh,<eng> it is (so); yes; <gai>cha’n eadh,<eng> it is not (so); no; <gai>an eadh?<eng> is it so? would you really? <gai>mar gu’m b’eadh,<eng> as if.


Page 15.

<gai>cho sgaiteach ’s as urrainn:<eng> read is for as; ’s stands for <gai>agus.

sàr-bhàird: sàr,<eng> excellent, always before its noun; so <gai>deagh,<eng> good; <gai>droch,<eng> bad. Some other adjectives, but only monosyllables, may be prefixed, of which <gai>sean<eng> is the commonest. All prefixed adjectives aspirate the noun qualified, if it admits of aspiration.

<gai>a bha dearbhadh …<eng> supply ag; after the verbal noun dearbhadh we should have had gen. <gai>fiughantais,<eng> were it not that the gen. <gai>birlinne<eng> depends on <gai>fiughantas<eng> itself. Such is the custom of modern

[TD 199]

Gaelic; the older language used the gen. in the case of both the dependent nouns, e.g., <gai>nighean fhir na Comraich,<eng> “the daughter of the Laird of Applecross.”

<gai>air a h-àirdead:<eng> for all its height; cf. <gai>air theothad nan deur,<eng> in spite of the heat of the tears, p. 20. Air caisead na leacainn cha saltradh i comhnard.—D. Macintyre.
For all the steepness of the hillside, she would not tread a flat.

<gai>nach robh aige: muir<eng> is sometimes mas., but read aice. In O.Ir. there were three genders, masculine, feminine, and neuter. When the neuter was dropped, neuter nouns had to become either mas. or fem., hence occasional differences in treatment, e.g., <gai>muileann,<eng> originally neuter, is now usually mas., but fem. in Lewis.

<gai>an laimh dheis:<eng> adj. inflected in dat. fem.

<gai>is leòir:<eng> as the triphthong eoi is always long, the accent here indicates not length, but merely that the o is open as in seòl, not close as in mór.

<gai>bhathas a’ ciallachadh:<eng> bhathas is past impersonal; “they were wont to mean”; so in Mid. Ir. in sluagtech i m-bádus oc tinól na slóg, “the meeting-house where the hosts were being assembled.” Eriu I., 54. do bághas ag denamh marbha ortha ar gach taobh, ib. I., 55. Cf. thathas ag radha, p. 31, note.

<gai>a’ Mhuileartach Bhuidhe:<eng> for the heroic ballad of the Muileartach, see Scottish Celtic Review, p. 115.

<gai>air an Fhéinn:<eng> nom. <gai>Fiann;<eng> gen. <gai>Féinne;<eng> dat. <gai>Féinn;<eng> originally “a warrior band”; in tradition, both Scottish and Irish, applied specially to the band or bands of which Fionn Mac Cumhail was chief. On p. 17, dat. an Fhéinn is now used as nom. (See Duanaire Fhinn, Irish Text Society; Fianaigecht, Royal Irish Academy; Todd Lecture Series, XVI.).

Albainn:<eng> nom. <gai>Alba,<eng> gen. <gai>Alban, na h-Alba;<eng> dat. <gai>Albainn.<eng> Alba was originally applied to the whole of Britain, and is used so up to the tenth century (cf. “perfidious Albion” still). But long before that time it had come to mean usually “Scotland.” (2) Scotland.

<gai>tha fios againn; cha’n ’eil fhios:<eng> the aspiration of fios in the second expression is due to a suppressed poss. pron. 3 per. sg. with prospective reference to the following clause: “cha’n ’eil a fhios a bheil té” … The prospective use of the poss. pron. is found in the earliest Gaelic literature. Cf. atá a fhios agamsa go ndénad daoine, etc. (Carswell, p. 224).

<gai>eadar-dhà-sgeul:<eng> better da: eadar does not aspirate in Scottish Gaelic except in the sense of “both,” e.g., <gai>“ghabh e Alba uile eadar mhonadh is mhachair,”<eng> “He took possession of all Alba, both high ground and plain.”

<gai>ars’ a muime:<eng> Ir. ar; E.Ir. ar; s of <gai>ars (arsa)<eng> has arisen from phrases like <gai>ar se,<eng> “said he.” It is not really part of the verb. Cf. also <gai>“ar san tres ughdar glic,”<eng> “said the third wise author,” where san is the full form of the article. See MacBain’s Dict.


Page 29.

<gai>fo spòig:<eng> nom. <gai>spòg;<eng> it is to be inferred that spòg is fem.

<gai>eucoir nan Gall:<eng> “of the Norsemen”; <gai>gall<eng> means (1) a native of Gaul, Lat. Gallus. In the early centuries of the Christian era there was intercourse and trade between Gaul and Ireland. Gaulish wine and mercenaries were well known to the Irish. (2) A Norseman after 800 A.D., when the Norse invasions began, e.g., Cogadh Gaedhel re Gallaibh (An Irish account of the Norse wars). (3) An English-speaking person from the lowlands.

<gai>bratach Shil-Leòid:<eng> the Fairy Banner of the Macleods preserved in Dunvegan.

<gai>lorg geoidh …<eng> with reference to a suggestion that h-Irt (St Kilda), a name which means “death,” was so called because it was regarded as the abode of departed spirits.

[TD 203]

Page 33.

The position taken is that (1) the Pictish language was a dialect of Old British. Old British is now represented by Welsh. (2) Gaelic was introduced into Scotland from Ireland in the early centuries of the Christian era

The Picts are regularly called <gai>Cruithne<eng> in Gaelic, Picti in Latin, Pechts in Old English. The Old Norse form is seen in Petlandsfjördr, Pictlands-firth, now Pentland Firth. Pict is not from Latin pictus, painted.


Page 35.

Gaelic in Lewis: Lewis was for a long time between circ. 850 and 1200 wholly Norse-speaking, or nearly so. What language did Norse supersede in Lewis? Gaelic or Pictish?

<gai>Na Cataich:<eng> “the Cat-folk”; i Cataib, among the Cat-folk, is the old expression for the modern Sutherland.


Page 37.

<gai>Oirthir nan Gàidheal:<eng> Argyll of old extended northwards to Loch Broom. The northern part was called <gai>an t-Oirthir mu thuath,<eng> and the southern part <gai>an t-Oirthir mu dheas.<eng>


Page 38.

<gai>Comraich Ma-Ruibhe:<eng> Malruba’s Sanctuary or Girth. Malruba, an Irish priest and noble, founded the monastery of Applecross in 673. He died in 722. He exercised great influence in the north and west. For Moluag and the others, see Index of Persons.


Page 40.

The circular structures referred to are found from Pitlochry to the Western sea. They resemble brochs in some ways, but they show important differences. For a full account see Proceedings of the Society of Antiquaries of Scotland, 1912-13 and 1915-16.

[TD 204]

<gai>Diarmad:<eng> the Scottish traditions relating to Diarmad and the other heroes of the Fionn-cycle have been collected by J. F. Campbell in West Highland Tales, and by J. G. Campbell in The Fians. The other great cycle of ancient Gaelic tales, the Cuchullin-cycle, referred to on p. 40, is represented among us chiefly by the tale of Deirdre and the Sons of Uisneach, for which see Dr A. Carmichael’s Deirdre.

<gai>a nis:<eng> = Ir. anois = Mid. Ir. anosa = E. Ir. innossai = O. Ir. indossa = ind, the, and fois, rest.

<gai>earball: = air,<eng> before, and <gai>ball,<eng> member; air is a common prefix, and appears in modern Gaelic also as ir-, or-, ur-, e.g., <gai>iorghuil = air<eng> and <gai>gal; òirdheirc = airdirc,<eng> from <gai>dearc,<eng> see; <gai>oirthir = air<eng> and <gai>tìr; urchair = air<eng> and <gai>cor,<eng> a throw; <gai>ùrlar = air<eng> and <gai>làr.<eng> It is also common in names of places, e.g., Urquhart, Urrard, Orchill—v. p. 34.

<gai>a nunn:<eng> = O.Ir. inunn = an, from, and sund, here.

<gai>a nall:<eng> = O.Ir. anall = an, from, and root of thall—v. MacBain’s Dict. The a here is therefore historically justified; it is often extended by analogy to <gai>sios<eng> and <gai>suas<eng> (a sios, &c.), without historical warrant.

[TD 205]

Page 48.

a: consider the exact meaning of a in every case that occurs on this page. Note (1) <gai>’g a leigeil,<eng> poss. pron. with prospective reference to the clause co dhiù … (2) <gai>a chur an céill,<eng> poss. pron. with retrospective reference, not to casaid (which is fem.), but to the whole idea, casaid laidir, &c.

<gai>air méis:<eng> nom. <gai>mias<eng> = Lat. mensa. The form of the dative shows it to be fem.


Page 49.

<gai>nios<eng> = E.Ir. anís = an, from, and <gai>ìos,<eng> below, as in <gai>uasal;<eng> so <gai>a nuas<eng> = an and uas, above, as in uasal. Thus <gai>a nios<eng> and <gai>a nuas<eng> are correct historically.

<gai>ion:<eng> for <gai>ionann.

is i cur crìch:<eng> in full <gai>ag cur crìche.

gun i dh’ fhosgladh:<eng> here dh’ (= do) is irrational, and better omitted.

<gai>teine Bhàail:<eng> see 2 Kings 2. 17, vv. 16, 17. See also p. 170 below. There was a Celtic god Belenos, “the bright one,” probably a sun-god, whose name is traditionally associated with Bealltuinn, May-day. On that day two need-fires <gai>(teine-éiginn)<eng> were lit between which cattle were driven for purification and luck. There is no connection between the Indo-European word Belenos, “bright one,” and Semitic Baal, “lord.”


Page 50.

<gai>Mu’m b’ urrainnear: urrainn<eng> or <gai>urradh<eng> is a noun, meaning “guarantee,” “authority.” Thus, (1) “cha’n urrainn mi sin a dheanamh” means, “I am not a sufficient authority for (a = do) doing that.” Consider <gai>“gus iomadh ni dheanamh, nach bhuil daoine anns an aimsir-sa ’nan urrainn a dhealbh ciamar a rinneadh iad,”<eng> p. 136. Alongside of this construction we find (2) <gai>“cha’n urrainn domh sin a dheanamh,”<eng> “(there is) not authority to me for doing that.” Here <gai>urrainn<eng> is abstract. The text shows (3) <gai>urrainnear<eng> as an impersonal verb. This, however, is by false analogy, and is

[TD 206]

not to be imitated. It is used in the dialect of Jura and neighbouring islands.

<gai>an tigh a réiteach:<eng> here a = do.

<gai>gus an<eng> = O.Ir. cossin = <gai>co,<eng> to, and <gai>sin,<eng> the, that. This is one of the cases where the primitive initial s of the article is preserved; cf. anns an, leis an, ris an.

<gai>uidh air n-uidh:<eng> lit. “distance after distance”; here <gai>air<eng> represents iar n-, after, the n of which appeared before a vowel and was written with the word following. So <gai>iochd air n-achd.<eng> The same thing is seen in the personal pronouns <gai>ar<eng> and <gai>bhur,<eng> e.g.,

[TD 207]

<gai>ar n-athair.<eng> The explanation is that these words ended originally in n; v. MacBain’s Dict.

<gai>cha ruigeadh i (a) leas:<eng> “she would not attain to its profit,” “to profit therefrom,” “it would not profit her”; hence “she need not have been,” &c.


Page 56.

<gai>ath-bhuaireadh:<eng> here <gai>ath<eng> is used as an adjective, meaning “next.” As a prefix it denotes:—
(1) back, again, Lat re- in various shades of meaning: e.g., <gai>ath-leasachadh,<eng> reformation; <gai>aicheamhail = athghabhail,<eng> revenge.
(2) done out, done with; Lat. ex-: e.g., <gai>atach = ath-aodach,<eng> worn-out clothes.
(3) very; e.g., <gai>ath-ghoirid,<eng> very short; <gai>ath-bhreac,<eng> very speckled.

<gai>de dh’ airgiod:<eng> dh’ is irrational.

<gai>a lòn-maidne ghabhail:<eng> supply a = do before ghabhail; <gai>ri duais fhaotainn,<eng> p. 57; <gai>solus fhaotainn,<eng> p. 59; and passim.

(1) abstract nouns: <gai>diadhachd, mórachd, fallanachd.<eng>
(2) quasi-collective nouns: <gai>rioghachd, baranachd, Gàidhealtachd;<eng> equivalent to English -dom.

<gai>sheinneadh e:<eng> the language of these rhythmical “runs” <gai>(ruitheanna)<eng> is often obscure. The runs are the oldest part of the tales, and their form was fixed so long ago that the wording became obscure to the reciters, who therefore were apt, while maintaining the general sound and the number of rhythmical b ats, to slur unfamiliar terms or modify them by analogy. For their understanding, a knowledge of the older stages of the language is useful, but there is a residuum in them that can only be guessed at.

<gai>uirt:<eng> pl. of <gai>ort<eng> .i. <gai>orgain,<eng> destruction. O’Davoren’s Glossary. This is confirmed by a variant in W.H.T., I., 302, <gai>“sheinneadh iad puirt agus uirt agus orgain.”

tairteil:<eng> ? Ir. tartamhail, thirst-provoking.

<gai>ainn:<eng> ? Ir. àin, .i. imáin, driving.

<gai>siogaidh:<eng> “a word applied to the motions of a serpent; hence, s applied to music, twining and coiling round the heart.” C.R., III., 356. But it may be Ir. <gai>síoguidhe<eng> (for <gai>síodhuidhe<eng>), a fairy; “fairy tune.”

<gai>a chridhe:<eng> for <gai>de chridhe.

<gai>ceangal nan trì chaoil:<eng> the three “smalls” are given as <gai>caol na coise, caol an duirn, caol an droma,<eng> ankle, wrist, neck (waist). This is the regular Scottish Gaelic phrase. Irish has usually ceangal na gcúig gcaol, viz., wrists, ankles, neck. For nan trì chaoil we should have expected n. t. caol. The Eigg version is n. t. caoil. The whole binding was fastened by one knot.

<gai>turlach:<eng> a large fire; a bonfire, H.S.D.; from O.Ir. ur, fire; Gr. πυρ; with prosthetic t, and suffix -lach, place of.

<gai>còtan caomh cotain:<eng> Ir. cotún means “a wadded or quilted tunic worn under the hauberk, originally made of cotton.” There appears to be some confusion here between cotún in this sense and còtan, a little coat. The original may have been a chotan caomh cotud; cotud, hard, strong.

<gai>mo dha chois … chluais:<eng> with <gai>dà,<eng> two, the old dual number is still used. It has now only one case, which is the same in form as the dative singular.

<gai>dheanadh iad creagan …<eng> a “run.’ Similar descriptions are not uncommon. In a very old tale the hero, Curoi, sends Cuchullin into the earth, first to his knees, then to his thighs, then to his girdle, and then to his arm-pits. (Celt. Zeitschrift, IX., p. 192).

<gai>bhuain e gad:<eng> so in Laoidh nan Ceann, Conall Cearnach, avenging Cuchullin’s death, strings the heads of Cuchullin’s foes on a withy <gai>(gad).<eng> Heads of enemies were counted as valuable trophies by the Continental Celts, and the custom appears frequently in the Old Irish sagas. Thus it is related of Cet Mac Màgach, a warrior of Connaught, “he went westwards having the heads of thrice nine men of Ulster with him” (trí nói cinn do Ultaib lais). Death-Tales of the Ulster Heroes: Todd Lecture XIV.

<gai>lorg a dhà shùla:<eng> the track of his two eyes; a straight line as the crow flies.

[TD 213]

the reference may be to the seed <gai>(fras),<eng> which appears on the very top of a plant. The term, however, is probably a corruption of the old term <gai>fas-mullach,<eng> “firm top.”

<gai>comhrag ciad,<eng> i.e., each of them would encounter a hundred, presumably one at a time.

<gai>tarbh truid,<eng> “a bull of combat,” “a furious bull”; <gai>trod<eng> is now restricted to a quarrel in words, scolding.

<gai>cailleach mhór:<eng> the idea of reviving slain men, so as to be fit to fight again, is very old, and appears in several tales, e.g., <gai>Fionn an Tigh a’ Bhlàir Bhuidhe.<eng> Compare the story of Medea and her magic cauldron of rejuvenation.


Page 93.

<gai>chuireadh e leith-bhàir: leith<eng> often refers to one of a pair of things that are usually found together, often with the suggestion that its fellow is deficient or absent, e.g., <gai>tha e air leith-chois, leith-shuil,<eng> &c. <gai>Leithallt<eng> in place-names is applied to a steep <gai>(allt)<eng> rising up from a narrow valley without a corresponding steep on the other side. Compare <gai>leitir = leith<eng> and <gai>tìr. Leith-bhàir<eng> means a goal at one end of the shinty field.

<gai>fàd-comhraig:<eng> “a battle sod.” <gai>Beum-sgéithe,<eng> “a blow on the shield,” meant as a challenge. For the <gai>fàd-còmhraig,<eng> compare the opening of the fight

[TD 214]

between Cuchullin and Etarcomol. “Cuchullin smites the sod that was under his (Etarcomol’s) feet, so that he fell at full length with his sod on his belly”—(“Benaid Cúchulaind in fót bói fo chossaib, co torchair inna lige 7 a fhót fora thairr).


Page 94.

<gai>cha robh cleas:<eng> a version of part of this run occurs in another Eigg tale:
<gai>Cha robh cleas a dheanadh sgiotaiche no sgoitiche
Le dìsnean ghillean-feall,
No le organ nam manach,
No le oilean nan damhach [dannach
Nach deanadh an gaisgeach:
Cleasa clise, cleasa fise,
Cleasa frise, cleasa gise,
Cleasa doilbhe doilleire,
Cleasa cleiteam cleasach an ògam.

sgithich, sgothaich:<eng> the forms <gai>sgiotaiche<eng> and <gai>sgoitiche<eng> are probably better; O’Reilly has <gai>sgoitiche,<eng> “mountebank,” and <gai>sgiot,<eng> “game,” “gibe.”

<gai>organ nam manach: organ<eng> is obscure; <gai>manach<eng> here means “trickster,” “juggler,” old <gai>“monach”; muine,<eng> “treacherous trick.”

<gai>cleas a’ chleiteam:<eng> a corruption of <gai>cleas cleténech,<eng> “javelin feat,” from <gai>cletíne,<eng> a javelin. It was one of the principal cleasa of the heroes.

<gai>cleas an òigeam:<eng> thought to be “ogham feat,” from O.Ir. ogam, the peculiar method of writing by means of strokes variously arranged, that was used in Ireland before the Roman alphabet was introduced. Cuchullin performs a feat that might be justly so termed, but it was one that he alone, of the men of his time, could perform, and the term <gai>cleas oguim<eng> does not appear. A further difficulty is that in the modern language the word is ogham, with g aspirated.

<gai>ubhal a’ chleasaiche:<eng> the <gai>ubhal cleas<eng> is often mentioned in the old sagas. It was performed with balls.

<gai>ditheannan dearge teine:<eng> “peelings” and “showers” are suggested for <gai>ditheannan.

[TD 215]

an cailceadh:<eng> In the ancient tale, Fled Bricrend, Bricriu’s Feast, occurs: “they hewed at one another till the one half of the palace was an atmosphere of fire with the swords and with the edges of the spears, and the other half was (like) a pure white bird-flock from the chalk of their shields” <gai>(cailc nan sgiath).<eng> In Cath Mucrama mention is made of “finn-nél na cailce ocus ind aeil dochum inna nél asnaib sciathaib ocus asna bocóitib ocá nesorgain de fhaebraib na claideb,” &c.: “a white cloud of chalk and lime (rising) to the clouds from the shields and bucklers smitten by the edges of the swords.” Silva Gadelica, p. 316. Dr Joyce says “hide-covered shields were often whitened with lime or chalk, which was allowed to dry and harden, as soldiers now pipeclay their belts.” Social Hist. of Anc. Ireland, I., 129.

<gai>chladhaich e toll:<eng> Sigurd slew the dragon Fafnir by digging a hole from which he smote upwards as Fafnir passed over it.


Page 95.

<gai>eadar dà sgeul …<eng> “by the way and between ourselves.”

<gai>cha’n iarr i seòl …<eng> compare what is said of the ships of the isle of Phaeacia, Odyssey, 8, 556: “that our ships may convey thee, hitting the way with their own wits. For the Phaeacians have no steersmen, nor yet have their ships rudders as have other ships, but of themselves they know the thoughts and minds of men, and they know the cities and fat lands of mankind, and most swiftly cross the salt sea gulf shrouded in cloud and mist.”
Note that though <gai>bàta<eng> is mas., the pronoun is fem.

<gai>cumaidh iad àirdeachd riut:<eng> “they will make a course for thee.”

<gai>beannachd na caoimh …<eng> “the blessing of friendship and of the sanctuary”—a common form of farewell in some of the northern isles.

<gai>uisge an easain:<eng> In one of the old ballads the lines occur:—
<gai>Cha laigh rosad orm a chaoidh.<eng>

<gai>an fhead chaol … “chluinneadh iad fead Chaoilte na b’fhaide na ruitheadh e fhéin o mhoch gu dubh,” agus b’ esan am fear bu luaithe de ’n Fhéinn.

feath nan eun fionn:<eng> a euphemism for a hurricane.

Page 97.

<gai>ri gualainn an rìgh:<eng> the place of honour; so in Fled Bricrend, Bricriu is seated for gúaluind Conchobair, at the shoulder of Concobar the king.

<gai>thar nan seachd sparranan:<eng> seven is a complete number. The houses of nobles among the Gael of Ireland were often of good height. The banqueting hall of Tara <gai>(Teamhar)<eng> has been ascertained to measure 759 feet long by 46 feet wide; it was at least 45 feet high. (Joyce’s Soc. Hist. of Ireland, II., p. 85).


Page 98.

<gai>ceannach:<eng> here “reward,” “price”; so in Irish.

<gai>chaidh . . trulainn:<eng> “went topsy-turvy.”


Page 99.

<gai>bearradh eoin is amadain:<eng> “this phrase is explained to mean clipping the hair and beard off one side of the head. The idea is taken from clipping the wing of a bird.” J. F. Campbell in W.H.T., II., 490. Close cropping or shaving the hair of the head was an intolerable insult, as being the symbol of thralldom. When Cuchullin had his hair shaved by Curoi, under circumstances of peculiar indignity, he shunned the sight of men for a whole year. One of the tales in S. H. O’Grady’s Silva Gadelica tells how the <gai>tuatha luchra<eng> (the Lilliputians) came to Emain Macha in Ulster to seek the release of their king, Iubdan. They threatened reprisals, viz., to let the calves all over Ulster get

[TD 217]

to their mothers, to burn every mill and kiln in Ulster, &c., and carried out their threats without effect. Finally they said: <gai>“berrfam fuilt bar mban agus bar bfer co mbéid fó méla agus fó aithis co bràch. dar mo bréithir, ar Fergus, dá ndéntáise sin muirbfetsa Iubdan.”<eng> “We will crop the hair of your women and of your men so that they shall be under reproach and under disgrace till doom. On my word, said Fergus, if you do that, I will slay Iubdan.” The tonsure of the monks was a symbol of their being servi Christi.

This fine tale should be compared with the tale entitled Eirig Fhinn in J. G. Campbell’s Fians, p. 250. The central idea of the tales is found in the version of the death of Cúroi as given by Keating, with which Eirig Fhinn has a distinct affinity.

The first battle of Inverlochy was fought in 1431 between the royal troops under the Earls of Mar and Caithness and the Macdonalds under Donald Balloch, son of John of Isla and cousin-german of of the Earl of Ross. See Gregory, Hist. of the Western Highlands, p. 37; Drummond Norie, Loyal Lochaber, pp. 25-28.

The story of O’Birrin is given in English, rather more fully, in Collectanea de Rebus Albanicis, p. 310, from an old MS. History of the Macdonalds. A’ Bhriagach is a little way beyond Achavady in Glenroy, on a burn called Allt na Briagaich; given wrongly in the MS. History of the Macdonalds as Baggech and Beggich. The site of O’Birrin’s house is still known.


Page 100.

<gai>’n a churaich:<eng> here <gai>curach<eng> is inflected in the dat. as if fem.; it is regularly mas.


Page 101.

<gai>am boicionn bràtha:<eng> the goatskin on which the quern was placed.

[TD 218]

Page 102.

<gai>is ionmhainn leam:<eng> The earl’s readiness in improvising marks him as a poet. Similarly many of the Norse leaders and warriors were skalds, and improvised in much the same style.


Page 107.

beavers: the beaver appears to have existed in Britain in historic times, indeed up, it is said, till about 1100; hence Beverley, Beverstone. Gaelic has no real name for “beaver,” <gai>los-leathan,<eng> “broad-tailed,” being merely descriptive and of doubtful authority. The old Celtic for “beaver” (*bebros) is generally agreed to appear in the Gaulish place-name Bibracte, and also in Bibroci, the name of a Celtic tribe in the south-east of Britain in Cæsar’s time. This term has not been recognised in any Scottish or Irish place-name.


Page 111.

<gai>a Mhuire naomh:<eng> note that the vocative case of fem. nouns and adjectives is always the same as the nominative.

<gai>Clachan nan Druidhneach:<eng> “the stone-house of the artificers” (no connection with Druids). The clachan, Ir. clochán, was a circular building of stone, of bee-hive shape. A specimen in good preservation exists on one of the islands called <gai>na h-Eileacha Naomha,<eng> between Scarba and Mull. Druidhneach is not uncommon in names of places, e.g., <gai>Cladh nan Druidneach, Eilean nan D.<eng> They apparently formed a sort of caste of themselves.


Page 112.

<gai>is i an eaglais bheag . . . .<eng> Dr Macleod no doubt gives the tradition of Iona on the point. There can be no historical certainty.

<gai>na h-aitreabh naoimhe:<eng> aitreabh is sometimes fem., usually mas.

[TD 219]

<gai>chum an dion:<eng> the protection would be rather against the north wind than against the Norsemen.

<gai>an t-Ollamh Sasunnach:<eng> Dr Samuel Johnson, who visited the Western Isles in 1773, and wrote a valuable description of his tour, entitled A Journey to the Western Islands of Scotland, in which occurs the passage here translated—“We were now treading that illustrious island,” &c.

<gai>I Chalum Chille:<eng> the best account of Iona is by the Rev. E. C. Trenholme—The Story of Iona; Edinburgh: D. Douglas, 1909 (xv. and 173 pp.; 40 full-page illustrations; drawings, maps, and plans. 8/6 net).

<gai>is ann an caisteal:<eng> the castle of Carnaburg, on one of the Treshnish Isles, an exceedingly strong fastness; its ruins remain.

<gai>leac an duine so:<eng> this tombstone, beautifully ornamented and bearing a galley, is depicted in The Story of Iona, plate 37.

<gai>Cha lean an sionnach air sior ruith:<eng> a proverb apparently omitted by Nicolson.

<gai>Is feárr sgur na sgàineadh:<eng> the reply is, <gai>“is feàrr sgàineadh fhéin na biadh maith fhagail.”<eng> See Sgeulachd Mhic-a-Rusgaich, W.H.T., II., 341.

<gai>an Greugach:<eng> ? Solon, the Athenian sage and legislator.

<gai>Mac-an-Tòisich:<eng> Donald Mackintosh was born at Orchilmore, near Blair-Atholl, in 1743. An account of him is given at p. 416 of Nicolson’s Gaelic Proverbs. His Collection of Gaelic Proverbs and Familiar

[TD 220]

Sayings was issued in 1785. In 1789 he was ordained Deacon in the Scottish Episcopal Church, and thereafter Priest. In 1801 he was appointed Gaelic Translator and Keeper of Gaelic Records to the Highland Society of Scotland, in which capacity he compiled catalogues of Gaelic MSS. belonging to the Highland Society and others, and transcribed some of the MSS. (Vol. III. of the London Highland Society’s Ossian, pp. 566-573). Donald Mackintosh died on 22nd Nov., 1808, and was buried in the Greyfriars’ Churchyard, Edinburgh, where also lies Duncan Bàn Macintyre. In his will he designated himself: “I, the Reverend Donald Mackintosh, a priest of the Old Scots Episcopal Church, and last of the non-jurant Clergy of Scotland.” See Dr Magnus Maclean’s Literature of the Highlands, p. 137.

<gai>an Siorramh Mac Neacail:<eng> Alexander Nicolson, M.A., LL.D., born at Husabost, Skye, in 1827; d. at Edinburgh, January, 1893; scholar and poet. His Gaelic Proverbs, now out of print and difficult to procure, was published in 1881 (Maclachlan & Stewart, Edinburgh). See Dr Maclean’s Literature of the Scottish Highlands, p. 170.

<gai>Mac a’ Phearsain:<eng> James Macpherson, b. at Ruthven, Kingussie, 1736; d. 1796. For account of his life and his “Ossian” see Dr Magnus Maclean’s Literature of the Highlands, pp. 69-90.

<gai>Iain MacCodrum:<eng> flourished in the middle of 18th century. He belonged to North Uist, and was bard (after Duncan MacRury) to Sir James Macdonald of Sleat, who died at Rome in 1766 at the age of 25. His duais consisted of the annual sum of £2 5s, with 5 bolls of meal, 5 stones of cheese, and a croft rent free for life.—Clan Donald, III., 95.

<gai>MacDhomhnaill:<eng> i.e., Macdonald of Sleat.

<gai>Mac Mhic Ailein:<eng> the patronymic of the head of the house of Clan Ranald.

<gai>dha mhac:<eng> i.e., <gai>do a mhac.

Cath Gairbheach:<eng> the battle of Harlaw, fought in 1411 by Donald, Lord of the Isles, in prosecution of his claim to the Earldom of Ross. Both sides claimed the victory. A manuscript History of the Macdonalds records: “Patrick, Earl of Tullibardine, said, as the other noblemen were talking of the battle of Harlaw, ‘We know that Macdonald had the victory, but the Governor had the printer.’” Collectanea de Rebus Albanicis, p. 302.


Page 136.

<gai>Albannaich an Fheinn:<eng> the institution known as <gai>an Fhiann<eng> existed both in Ireland and in Scotland. They were organised bands of warriors under the leadership of a chief who was called <gai>Righ-féinne.<eng> The most celebrated of these leaders was Fionn Mac Cumhail, reputed to have lived in the third century, and his fame has attracted to him a great body of heroic tales.

<gai>uachdaran na Ròimhe:<eng> whether this is a genuine tradition or merely a literary revival, it is true that bands of Fiann did harass Roman Britain, as appears from the following extract, which refers to circ. A.D. 364:—“Eo tempore Picti in duas gentes divisi, Dicalydonas et Verturiones, itidemque Attacotti, bellicosa hominum natio, et Scotti per

[TD 222]

diversa vagantes multa populabantur.”—Ammianus Marcellinus.


Page 137.

<gai>I Cholum Chille:<eng> Columba settled in Iona in 563 A.D. It was first plundered by the Norsemen in 802, and in 850 the relics of Columba were removed by King Kenneth MacAlpin to Dunkeld. Of the original wattle-constructed monastery there are now no remains. At the beginning of the 13th century, Reginald, son of Somerled, established Benedictines <gai>(manaich dubha)<eng> in Iona; v. p. 145. The cathedral, which still stands, was dedicated to St Mary. The south-east pier of the tower bears the inscription, now mutilated, DONALDUS O’BROLCHAN FECIT HOC OPUS. The death of “Donald O’Brolchan, a prior,” is recorded in the Annals of Ulster for 1203. See further The Story of Iona.


Page 138.

<gai>an t-seann lamh sgrìobhaidh:<eng> this method of writing, still used in Ireland, was taken from the Romans, and had nothing distinctively <gai>Gàidhealach<eng> about it. The distinctively Gaelic writing was the Ogham, but this peculiar style was arrested, most probably through the hostility of the Church, which regarded it as pagan. See The Irish Ogham Inscriptions, by John Macneill (Hodge, Figgins, & Co., Dublin, 1909).

<gai>Ruairidh Mór:<eng> Sir Roderick Macleod of Harris and Dunvegan, chief 1590-1626; the most famous chief of the Macleods.


Page 139.

<gai>Lachlann mac Néill:<eng> this fine example of Gaelic genealogy covers eight generations; reckoning a generation at 30 years, it goes back for 240 years. If Lachlann was eighteenth in descent from Muireach, the latter would have flourished about the year 1260, which brings us approximately to the date of Muireach Albannach, the famous Irish poet,

[TD 223]

who was banished to Alba, and is claimed as the ancestor of the Mac Mhuirich bards. v. O’Curry, III., 280.

<gai>glùn:<eng> here “generation.” Ang.-Sax. cneo also means (1) knee, (2) generation.—Skeat.


Page 140.

<gai>an leabhar dearg:<eng> v. p. 143. The contents of the Red Book and the Black Book of Clanranald are published, with translation, in Reliquiæ Celticæ, vol. ii.

<gai>Raonull a mhac:<eng> Ranald Macdonald (Rao’ll Dubh), son of Alexander Macdonald, best known as Alasdair mac Mhaighistir Alasdair. Ranald published an excellent collection of Gaelic poetry: “Comh-Chruinneachidh Orannaigh Gaidhealach, le Raonuill MacDhomhnuill ann ’n Eilean Eigg. Duneidiunn: clo bhuailt ann le Walter Ruddiman, M,DCC,LXXVI.” He was schoolmaster of Eigg. His family emigrated to Canada, and Eigg tradition has it that the manuscripts referred to in the text went with them.

<gai>atáid:<eng> 3 pl. pr. ind. of <gai>atáim,<eng> “tha mi.”

<gai>taitneamhach:<eng> formed from <gai>taitneamh,<eng> act of shining on; act of pleasing; Sc. Gael. <gai>taitneach.<eng>
      
<gai>naomhtha:<eng> regularly used in Irish where we <gai>naomh.<eng> In Ir. <gai>naomh<eng> is used as a prefix except in the phrase <gai>an Spiorad naomh<eng> (Dineen’s Ir. Dict.).

<gai>ioc-shlainteamhail:<eng> the suffix here is O.Ir. sam(a)il, modern <gai>samhail,<eng> likeness. In Sc. Gael. it is reduced to <gai>ail, eil.

an leabhar Psalm-so Dhaibhioth:<eng> a good old idiom, now disused; seen, e.g., in <gai>anns a’ choille sin Crois<eng> (Beauties, p. 161a).

<gai>do sheinm:<eng> “to sound”; <gai>seinm,<eng> g. <gai>seanma,<eng> noun mas., “the act of playing music”; Sc. Gael, <gai>seinn.

d’ ar brosnughadh:<eng> for ag ar br.; there is confusion here between do and ag, and improper use of do.

[TD 224]

<gai>a leigmhail:<eng> verbal n. of <gai>leig,<eng> allow, now <gai>leigeil;<eng> O.Ir. léiced; Mod. Ir. leigean.

<gai>na n-gcreidmheacha:<eng> here initial c is pronounced g (written gc) under the influence of the final n of the previous word. This is called “eclipsis”; see Appendix B.

<gai>spioradadha,<eng> formed like O.Ir. corpdae; cf. <gai>aimsiordha,<eng> “temporal,” from <gai>aimsir; grianda,<eng> sunny; <gai>contrardha,<eng> contrary. The usual adj. from <gai>spiorad<eng> is <gai>spioradalta,<eng> as above on this page.

<gai>do ghabhas:<eng> 1 sg. pret. of <gai>gabhaim,<eng> I take.

<gai>achd gu:<eng> “save only that.”

<gai>an t-saothairigh:<eng> Mod. Ir. saothruidhe, a labourer; a provider; an earnest worker.

<gai>go sbailp(e) bréige:<eng> “with false self-esteem”; go, O.Ir. co, prep. “with”; Lat. cum; Sc. Gael. now uses <gai>le<eng> instead of it, as Kirk does in his next clause.

<gai>le gcroidhibh: le<eng> ought not to eclipse a succeeding consonant, for it did not end originally in n. Probably it is here made to eclipse by analogy with co(n), which did end in n.

<gai>diagha:<eng> better <gai>diadha;<eng> formed like <gai>aimsiordha,<eng> &c.

Somerled, regulus of Argyll, was son of Gille-Brìghde, son of Gille-Adamnan. He was killed in 1164, most probably by treachery and not in battle, in course of an expedition up the Clyde with 166 galleys. Somerled was the founder of the great family of de Insulis, or Macdonald, Lord of the Isles. He married Ragnhildis, daughter of Olaf the Red, King of Man and the Isles, and their three sons were Dugall, Reginald, and Angus. On Somerled’s death his great possessions were divided. Dugall got Lorn, Morvern, and Mull; Reginald got Kintyre, Cowall and Islay; Angus got lands further north, of which he and his family were dispossessed by Reginald.

The following table shows the relationship of the personages mentioned by MacVurich. It will be seen that he is not always quite correct:—

[TD 225]

GODFRED CROVAN, King of Man, etc, d. 1095 in Islay.

OLAVE the Red, King		m.	AFFRICA, d. of Fergus,
of Man, etc.,					Lord of Galloway.
killed 1154.

GODFRED the Black, King			*Ragnhildis, m. Somerled.
of Man, killed 1187.

Reginald, King	Olave the Black,	Dugall.		Reginald,	Angus,
of Man, etc.		King of Man, etc.,			d.		killed
killed 1229.		d. 1237.				1207.		1210.

The genealogy of Somerled is given by MacVurich as follows, and appears to be correct for three or four generations at least:—Somerled son of Gille Brìde son of Gille Oghamhnan [Adamnan] son of Solamh son of Merghach son of Niallgus son of Suibhne, and so on up to Coll Uais, grandson of Cormac Mac Airt, High King of Ireland, d. 277 A.D. Merghach (otherwise Imergi) is equated by Skene with Iehmarc, who did homage to Canute in 1029.

For the history of Somerled and his family see Clan Donald, vol. I.; Gregory’s Western Highlands; Skene’s Celtic Scotland, vol. III.; Skene’s Highlanders, ed. Dr MacBain, pp. 409, 410.


Page l44.

Instances of eclipsis are:—
a bhfogas for a nfogas; Sc. Gael. <gai>am fagus:<eng> bhf is sounded v.
go ttugadh for go ntugadh; Sc. Gael. <gai>gu’n tugadh;<eng> tt is sounded d; so, go ttug.
na bhferann for na nferann; Sc. Gael, <gai>nam ferann;<eng> bhf is sounded v, as above.
a ccoilltibh for a ncoilltibh; Sc. Gael. <gai>an coilltibh;<eng> cc is sounded g.
na bhfiadhnuis for ’na mfiadhnuis; Sc. Gael. <gai>’nam fianuis.<eng>


* It is not certain who was Ragnhildis’ mother.

[TD 226]

<gai>do leigenn:<eng> vb. n. of <gai>leigim,<eng> I let, allow.

<gai>do ghluais:<eng> the preterite or past tense often takes do. The initial consonant of the verb is aspirated, if it admits of aspiration, but only in the active voice, not in the passive.

<gai>tangadar:<eng> 3 pl. pret. of <gai>tigim;<eng> so, <gai>rabhadar, shaoiladar,<eng> &c.

<gai>tugsad:<eng> 3 pl. pret. of <gai>do-bheirim;<eng> also, <gai>tugadar.

tangas:<eng> 3 sg. pret. of <gai>tigim.

targes:<eng> for <gai>thairngeas,<eng> 3 sg. pret. of <gai>tairngim,<eng> I draw.

<gai>ar mhair:<eng> “that which remained,” “the remnant”; <gai>ar = a ro,<eng> in practice a rel. pron., used with past tense, e.g., <gai>ar mhairbh sé,<eng> all whom he killed.

<gai>ionnas nár phill:<eng> “so that he did not return”; <gai>nár = ná<eng> (negative) and <gai>ro;<eng> so <gai>nachar = nach<eng> and <gai>ro.

sgach aonlathair:<eng> in each single place; in Ir. <gai>lathair<eng> means a place, site, spot; place of meeting, presence, &c.; in Sc. Gael. the meaning is practically confined to “presence”: we use <gai>àite<eng> for the other meanings.


Page 145.

<gai>ré shluagh:<eng> “In modern Ir. <gai>ré<eng> is confused with <gai>le,<eng> and in modern MSS. they are often written indiscriminately”—Dinneen. This confusion never occurs in Sc. Gael. In previous line ré is used correctly = Sc. Gael. <gai>ri.<eng>

<gai>gur<eng> = <gai>gu<eng> and <gai>ro,<eng> whence the aspiration after it.

<gai>do cheannsaigh:<eng> note the absence of the pronoun e, which in Sc. Gael, would be present. This is characteristic of Irish, when the subject has been already expressed.

<gai>manach dubh:<eng> i.e., Benedictines.

<gai>manach liath:<eng> i.e., Greyfriars.

<gai>do chumhdaigh ord,<eng> &c.: “who founded the monastic order of Mo-lais.” St Mo-lais was connected with Holy Island, which guards Lamlash Bay, in Arran. Nothing further is known to me of this order founded, or equipped, by Reginald.

ri Alasdair: Alexander I. (1107-1124), “an astute and resolved prince, an independent patron of the

[TD 227]

Church, and the first to introduce charters north of Forth.”—Lang, Hist. of Scot., I., 101.


Page l46.

<gai>do marbhadh: do<eng> aspirates the pret. act. but not the pret. pass.

<gai>tangadar teachda …<eng> It is unlikely that Tara had much, if any, real influence on the succession. This Donald is <gai>Domhnall a h-Ile,<eng> Donald of Islay, from whom the Macdonalds or Clan Donald are called. He made the pilgrimage to Rome, and d. circ. 1249.

<gai>Aonghus Mór:<eng> succeeded circ. 1249, d. circ. 1294 (not 1234 as MacVurich has it). In his time the battle of Largs was fought, 1263; the Isles were formally ceded by Norway to Scotland in 1266. The Norsemen of the Isles were permitted either to emigrate to Norway or to remain as subjects of the King of Scotland.

<gai>Sliochd Dubhghoill mic Shomhuirle:<eng> the Macdougalls of Lorne.

<gai>Clann Eoin Aird na Murchann:<eng> John, s. of Angus, was called Eoin Sprangach. He is the eponymus of the MacIans of Ardnamurchan.

<gai>Inbher Feothfaramh:<eng> properly <gai>Inbher Pheofharan,<eng> Inver-peffry, i.e., Dingwall. MacVurich is not always sound on east-coast names.

<gai>Clann Alasdair:<eng> of Kintyre. The authors of Clan Donald consider Clann Alasdair to be named from Alasdair mac Domhnaill, brother of Aonghus Mór, and not from Alasdair mac Aonghuis as MacVurich has it.

<gai>Aonghus óg:<eng> Clan Donald makes Aonghus Mór succeeded by his son Alasdair, d. 1308. Then to him succeeded his brother Aonghus Og, who fought for Scotland at Bannockburn, and is celebrated in Scott’s Lord of the Isles. His wife was daughter of Connbuidh O’Cathan (O’Kane), one of the great barons of Ulster, Lord of Limavady and of the county of Derry. Her <gai>sochraid nemhghnáthach,<eng> or unusual retinue, have left descendants who are still known in the Highlands as <gai>tochradh nighean

[TD 228]

a’ Chathanaich<eng>—(Clan Donald, I., 100). Angus died circ. 1330, not in 1306.

<gai>an sliochd sin Raghnaill:<eng> v. note on p. 142, <gai>“an leabhar Psalm-so Dhaibhioth”;<eng> the idiom recurs.


Page 147.

<gai>cethrar ar fhichead:<eng> four on twenty; this is the correct form.

<gai>ór shiolaigh: ór = ó<eng> and <gai>ro<eng> of <gai>nár, ar,<eng> p. 144; <gai>ro<eng> is used with the past tense as well as <gai>do,<eng> and aspirates in active.

<gai>Eoin mac Aonghuis:<eng> “the good John of Isla”; chief 1330-1386; made a treaty with Edward Balliol, 1335; forfeited in 1343, and pardoned; fought on the side of the Black Prince at the battle of Poictiers, 1356, and was taken prisoner; released in 1357. Rendered allegiance to David II. at Inverness, 1369; married (1) his third cousin, Euphemia or Amy MacRuari, sister of Reginald MacRuari of Garmoran. He divorced or abandoned her (MacVurich says he never married her), and married (2) Margaret, daughter of Robert the Steward of Scotland, afterwards Robert II.

<gai>do rinne beatha:<eng> “he made a provision.”

<gai>a bfuil:<eng> for <gai>a nfuil;<eng> eclipsis.

<gai>gus an Bhélleith:<eng> unknown.

<gai>Domhnall a h-Ile:<eng> chief 1386—circ. 1423; he dealt generously with his brothers, the sons of his father’s first wife. He was educated at Oxford, and was familiar with the English Court. His claim to the Earldom of Ross was in right of his wife, Lady Margaret Leslie, sister of Alexander Leslie, Earl of Ross, d. 1402. He left an only daughter, Euphemia, who became a nun. Lady Margaret Lesley, wife of Donald, Lord of the Isles, was the next heir to the Earldom. The possessions of the Earldom lay in the counties of Ross, Inverness, Nairn, and Aberdeen, and it was plainly in pursuance of his claim thereto that the Lord of the Isles marched into Ross and eastwards to Aberdeenshire. For Harlaw see p. 135.

[TD 229]

<gai>Alasdair Carrach:<eng> son of John of Isla and Margaret, and brother of Donald of Harlaw. He got a grant of Lochaber from his father, and was the ancestor of the Macdonalds of Keppoch; fought at Harlaw, and at Inverlochy, where his archers helped to defeat the royal forces under the Earl of Mar, in 1431. For this his lands were forfeited and given to Mackintosh, chief of Clan Chattan, who had fought on the other side. The Macdonalds of Keppoch never afterwards held title. Date of Alasdair Carrach’s death unknown.
The poem of <gai>A’ Chomhachag,<eng> composed about 1600, refers to him thus:—

<gai>Chunnaic mi Alasdair Carrach,
An duine as allaile bha ’n Albainn;
Is minig a bha mi ’ga éisteachd
Is e ag réiteach nan tom sealga.

do b’fher connbhala …<eng> for <gai>congbhála;<eng> “the foresaid Lord was a supporter of churchmen and monks and priests continually in his retinue” (wrongly translated in Rel. Celt., II., 161, “for the better upholding of the monks and priests this lord kept in his company”).

<gai>mainisdir na croch naomh:<eng> “Holyrood Abbey”; the priory of Oransay, near Colonsay, is meant.—Clan Donald, I., 470.

<gai>teampull Odhran:<eng> the genitive of nouns in -án is very often written -án. Even at the present day the genitive of names of saints in -an is often pronounced -an, not -ain.

<gai>slat an tighernais:<eng> the investiture of the Lord of the Isles is thus described by Hugh Macdonald (circ. 1660): “The Bishop of Argyll, the Bishop of the Isles, and seven priests were sometimes present; but a Bishop was always present, with the chieftains of all the principal families, and a Ruler of the Isles. There was a square stone, seven or eight feet long, and the trace of a man’s foot cut thereon, upon which he stood, denoting that he should walk in the footsteps and uprightness of his predecessors,

[TD 230]

and that he was installed by right in his possessions. He was clothed in a white habit to show his innocence and integrity of heart, that he would be a light to his people, and maintain the true religion. The white apparel did afterwards belong to the poet by right. Then he was to receive a white rod in his hand, intimating that he had power to rule, not with tyranny and partiality, but with discretion and sincerity. Then he received his forefathers’ sword, or some other sword, signifying that his duty was to protect and defend them from the incursions of their enemies in peace or war. The ceremony being over, mass was said after the blessing of the bishop and seven priests.”—Collect. de Rebus Albanicis, p. 296.

<gai>tír unga:<eng> “an ounce land,” i.e., land for which an ounce of silver was paid as rent or tax. The land was further subdivided into pennylands, half-penny lands, and even farthing lands. Unganab (the Abbot’s ounce-land) in Uist was a twenty-four penny land, and is probably the land referred to in the text. The <gai>tír unga,<eng> or, as it appears in charters, “terung,” was the equivalent of a davach, a measure which varied in extent from one to four ploughgates according to soil and locality. A ploughgate was 104 acres. See Dr E. Beveridge’s North Uist, p. 41.


Page 149.

<gai>cuireadh an cenn do Diùc Murchadh: do<eng> for <gai>de<eng> as regularly. Murdoch, Duke of Albany, and his sons were executed in 1425. Alexander, Lord of the Isles, sat upon the jury which condemned them—see Gregory, W.H., p. 33.

<gai>mainisdir Ie:<eng> note gen. inflection of <gai>I,<eng> which is still heard in Argyll.

<gai>brathres an úird:<eng> i.e., of the order of St Benedict, <gai>manaich dubha.

a ccríochthe:<eng> for <gai>a ncríochthe,<eng> their bounds.

[TD 231]

<gai>Alasdar a mhac:<eng> Lord of the Isles, 1425-1449. He wae Earl of Ross practically, but was not recognised as such till about 1437. He married, not Margaret Livingston, but Elizabeth Seton, d. of Sir Alexander Seton, Lord of Gordon and Huntly. Died at Dingwall, and was buried at Chanonry of Ross on 8th May, 1449.—Chronicle of Earls of Ross.

<gai>Eoin a h-Ile mac Alasdair:<eng> Earl of Ross and Lord of the Isles, 1449-1494; concluded an independent treaty with Edward IV. of England at Ardtornish in 1461; attainted and forfeited 1475; pardoned 1476; forfeited and deprived of title and estates in 1494; retired to the monastery of Paisley; died circ. 1498; the last Lord of the Isles and Earl of Ross. See Gregory, W.H., pp. 41-58, and for extended account Clan Donald, vol. I.

<gai>Aonghus óg mac Eoin:<eng> circ. 1450-1490; natural son of John, Earl of Ross and Lord of the Isles; invaded Ross in 1481, and fought battle of “Lagabraad,” i.e., Lagaidh Brìde, otherwise Logie Wester, now merged in the parish of Urquhart, in the Black Isle, Ross; fought battle of <gai>Bàgh na Fola,<eng> or Bloody Bay, near Tobermory, circ. 1484, against an expedition organised by the Earls of Argyll and Athole; raided Athole circ. 1485; killed at Inverness 1490. He was the hero of the people, for his courage and generosity. The Irish Annals of Loch Ce chronicle his death thus:—
“MacDomhnaill of Alba, the best man in Erin or in Alba of his time, was unfortunately slain by an Irish harper, Diarmad Cairbrech, in his own chamber.”
He was about 40 at the time of his death.

<gai>armuinn Innsi Gall:<eng> from O.N. ármaðr, a steward, especially of royal estates. Here <gai>àrmuinn<eng> is synonymous with <gai>uaisle,<eng> nobles, elsewhere used by MacVurich. It occurs in the same sense in the Book of the Dean of Lismore (1512-1526):
<gai>lochlynych is armyn eaid<eng>
Norsemen and nobles they.

<gai>a ccionn<eng> .i. <gai>a ncionn;<eng> cc = g.

<gai>eider abhainn Fhada agus alta na Sionnach:<eng> i.e., Knapdale in north of Kintyre. <gai>Abhainn Fhada<eng> flows by Dunadd, the ancient hill-fort capital of Dalriada.

<gai>Eoin Muideordach mac Alasdair:<eng> (This and the following paragraph refer to chiefs of Clan Ranald): John of Moidart, Captain of Clan Ranald, illegitimate son of Alasdar mac Alain mic Ruairidh, chief of Clan Ranald; acknowledged by the clan as chief on his father’s death, circ. 1544; an able man, and led a stirring life. Note that he succeeded in transmitting the chiefship to his descendants. According to Clan Donald, II., p. 288, he died in 1584.

<gai>Blár Léine:<eng> reputed to mean “the battle of the shirts”; really “battle of Leny.” The battle was fought in defence of John of Moidart’s claims against those of Ronald Gallda.

<gai>Ailin mac Eoin Mhúideordaigh:<eng> died in 1593. He was not quite the model that MacVurich makes him out to have been.

This is a valuable list of leading Gaels of the seventeenth century:—

<gai>Raghnall óg mac Raghnall Arannaig:<eng> 2nd Earl and 1st Marquis of Antrim, b. 1610, d. 1682, son of Raghnall Arranach, 1st Earl of Antrim, so called because he was fostered in the isle of Arran. The family were descended from Sorley Buidhe, a son of Alexander of Dun-naomhaig in Islay.
The Macdonalds got large possessions in Antrim through the marriage of John of Dun-naomhaig, second son of John, Lord of the Isles, with Margery Bissett, heiress of the Glens in Antrim. Their son was Donald Ballach, d. 1476. He was succeeded by his son John, who, along with his son John

[TD 233]

Cathanach, was hanged on the Boroughmuir of Edinburgh in 1494. Alexander, son of John Cathanach, succeeded, and died in 1538. He was succeeded by his son James, d. 1565. Sorley Buidhe was a younger brother of James, and the ancestor of the Earls of Antrim, as stated above.
See notes on p. 154 for Alasdair Mac Colla’s connection.

<gai>Gille-esbuig Caoch:<eng> Marquis of Argyll, beheaded in 1661.

<gai>Sir Lachlunn mac GhilleEoin:<eng> died 1662.

<gai>Eoin Múideartach:<eng> Chief of Clan Ranald, 1619-1670.

<gai>Eoin mac Ruaidhridh mhóir:<eng> John Macleod of Harris and Dunvegan, chief 1626-1649.

<gai>Sir Domhnall Gorm:<eng> of Sleat, chief 1616-1643.

<gai>Niall an Chaislein:<eng> MacNeill of Barra; dates uncertain.

<gai>Lachlunn mac Eoin bhailbh:<eng> b. 1628, d. circ. 1700; his father was killed in 1641. See Celt. Rev., IV., 38.

<gai>Eoin garbh mac Ghille Choluim:<eng> acceded in 1648; drowned in 1671.—(Macdonald Collection of Gaelic Poetry, p. xxix.). “The most powerful and best built Highlander of his day.”—(Hist. of Macleods, A. Mackenzie).

<gai>Eoin garbh mac Eoin Abraich:<eng> acceded 1642; <gai>Mac Eoin Abraich<eng> was the patronymic of the Lairds of Coll.

<gai>Muirchadh mac Ghille Eoin:<eng> of Lochbuy; d. 1663.

<gai>Domhnall mac Aonghuis:<eng> chief of Glengarry, 1575-1635. His grandson Angus, who succeeded him, was made Lord Macdonald and Aros by Charles II.; d. 1680.

<gai>Ailin mac Dhomhnaill duibh:<eng> chief of the Camerons, 1608-1650, succeeded by his grandson Eoghan. See Celt. Rev., V., 75, 76.

<gai>Seórus donn mac Choinnigh óig:<eng> chief of the Mackenzies, 1633-1651.

[TD 234]

<gai>Domhnall dúabháil mac Aodha:<eng> chief of Clan Morgan, i.e., the Mackays, 1614-1649; made Lord Reay <gai>(morbhair Meghrath)<eng> by Charles I. in 1628—see The Book of Mackay.

The wars of Montrose are dealt with in Sir Walter Scott’s Legend of Montrose, Dr Neil Munro’s John Splendid, and Mr J. A. Steuart’s Red Reaper.

bárdagha:<eng> a garrison; in Gaelic there are three different words <gai>bàrd:<eng> (1) a poet, Gaulish bardos; native Celtic; (2) an enlosed meadow, paddock—a loan word from Scottish ward, a paddock, as in “calves’ ward”; (3) a guard—a loan from Eng. ward, as in “watch and ward.” In the second sense <gai>bàrd<eng> is very common in place-names north of the Grampians.

<gai>Loch Alann:<eng> Loch Aline. Locate all these places on a map.

<gai>blár an Afalla:<eng> Blair in Athol. Athole is now pronounced Athal, or perhaps better Adhal (there being little or no h-sound heard as there should be with the spelling th). MacVurich’s spelling recalls the ancient Athfoithle—v. Index of Names of Places.

[TD 235]

<gai>Alasdar mac Colla<eng> mic Ghille-easbuig mic Colla (d. 1558) mic Alasdair (d. 1538) mic Eoin Cathanaich (d. 1494), of the family of Dun-naomhaig (Dunnyveg) in Islay. Alasdar’s father, Coll mac Ghille-easbuig, is best known as Coll Ciotach, or the left-handed, from the fact that he could use his left hand as well as his right—Anglicised Colkitto. See, for an account of him, Clan Donald, II., 593. His son, Alasdair, was the most redoubtable warrior of his day, a man of the type of the righ Féinne of old. Some of the finest poems in the language were composed in his honour. Born in Colonsay early in 17th century; banished therefrom in 1639 by Gille-easbuig Gruamach, he went to relatives in Antrim; fought for the Catholic cause in Ireland; joined Montrose in 1644; fought at Tippermuir, Crathes, Inverlochy, Auldearn, Kilsyth; acting independently of Montrose in Kintyre, was defeated by General Leslie; whereon, after throwing garrisons into Dun Abhartaigh (Dunaverty) and Dun Naomhaig, he sailed for Ireland. Slain in battle in Co. Cork, 1647. See Clan Donald, III., 596-609.

<gai>on gar do Phert:<eng> Tippermuir.

<gai>ag Obuirdhegh:<eng> Crathes, about 14 miles from Aberdeen. <gai>Obuirdhegh<eng> is for <gai>Obair-dheadhain.<eng>

<gai>bráighe:<eng> “upper part” of places; seen in <gai>Bràigh Mharr,<eng> Braemar; <gai>Bràigh Mhoraibh,<eng> Brae Moray; <gai>Bràigh Loch-abar;<eng> <gai>Bràigh Chat,<eng> and very many other names. Opposed to <gai>bun<eng> or <gai>iochdar,<eng> Scottish laigh.

<gai>Apuinn an Mhéinneinigh:<eng> Appin (i.e., abbey-land) of the Menzies, so called from its having been the patrimony of the ancient Church of Dull, near Aberfeldy. The other well-known Appin is <gai>Apuinn mhic Iain Stiubhairt,<eng> between Loch Leven and Loch Creran. It belonged to the Church of Lismore.

<gai>Clann Ghriogoir:<eng> in the Dean of Lismore’s Book, MacGregor is called <gai>“Seabhag deudgheal nan trì ghleann,”<eng> i.e., Glen Lyon, Glen Strae, and Glen Orchy. The MacGregors were gradually dispossessed by the Campbells.

<gai>clann an Aba:<eng> the Macnabs of Glen Dochart, at the west end of Loch Tay. They claim descent from the lay abbot of Glen Dochart. They were bought out by the Campbells. Their ancient burial-place is <gai>Innis Bhuidhe<eng> in the river Dochart, at Killin.

<gai>fir an brághad: brághad,<eng> gen. of <gai>bráighe,<eng> “higher part.” The Macgregors and Macnabs are meant—i.e., the men of <gai>Bràghaid Albainn,<eng> Breadalbane.

<gai>tigherna Achaidh na mBreac:<eng> Duncan Campbell of Achinbreck was a cousin of Argyll’s. He was slain at Inverlochy by Alasdair Mac Colla. The following verses with reference to this exploit are from “Oran Luathaidh do Alasdair MacColla,” Macdonald Collection, p. 40:—

morbhair Oglabi:<eng> James, 7th Lord Ogilvie, created Earl of Airlie, etc., 1639; a zealous adherent of Charles I. His son, Sir Thomas, fell at Inverlochy.

[TD 237]

Page 157.

<gai>láirc Thurraid:<eng> Glen Turret is at the head of Glen Roy, and <gai>Làirig Thuraid<eng> is otherwise known as <gai>an Làirig Mhór.<eng> Montrose started from near Loch Uanagan, marched southwards by the knoll called <gai>Glùn Chuil-eachaidh,<eng> and turning to the right at the first burn (about a mile from his starting point), followed the course of the burn, and so on to the <gai>Làirig Mhór,<eng> which leads in to Glen Turret. The march was hard enough, but its difficulty has been wildly exaggerated by making Montrose lead his men “up the course of the Tarff to the sources of the Spey, and, thence, across the hills into Glen Roy”—the Corrieyairack route. It is also stated that, on crossing the Spean, “after traversing the precipitous mountains over which Ben Nevis reigns supreme, he found himself late at night in Glen Nevis.”—(Loyal Lochaber, p. 56). That would have been a mad route. Montrose, being a man of sense, and having excellent guides, took the most direct way, which, indeed, was formidable for some miles at the high part of <gai>Làirig Thuraid,<eng> but before and after that part it presented no special difficulty. As it was, Montrose’s men marched nearly forty miles in deep snow and with little food, crossing a high and wild pass in course of their march.

<gai>tarrla partaidh don námhaid:<eng> no doubt a plundering party; cf. <gai>“loisgeadar bráigh Loch h-Abar,”<eng> p. 156. Iain Lom Macdonald in his poem <gai>Latha Inbhir-Lòchaidh<eng> says:—

<gai>Dìreadh a mach Glùn Chuil-eachaidh
Dh’ aithnich mi oirbh sùrd bhur tapaidh;
Ged bha mo dhùthaich ’na lasair
Is éirig air a’ chùis mar thachair.

bun Neibheis:<eng> where the river Nevis joins the Lochy; so Iain Lom:—

<gai>Cormac mac Airt,<eng> husband of Ethne and father of Cairbre, was the most illustrious of the pagan kings of Ireland. He was grandson of Conn Cédchathach. In his time, according to tradition, flourished Fionn MacCumhail, who was his son-in-law. Cormac reigned A.D. 254-266, when he abdicated, and died in 277.

<gai>Cairbre Lithfeachair:<eng> Cairbre of the Liffey succeeded Cormac. Defeated and broke the rebellious Fiann at the battle of Gabhra. The ending -car here = “fond of,” e.g. <gai>conchar,<eng> “fond of hounds.” There is also -car -cor, -cur, a setting, a casting, e.g. <gai>crannchar.

i Laighnibh:<eng> “in Leinster”; literally “among the Lagenians”; cf. <gai>i cataib,<eng> in Sutherland.

[TD 240]

Lughaidh Lamhfhada:<eng> He was really a god of the ancient Gael; Welsh Lleu. He was a god of light, art and science, and as such corresponds partly to Apollo, partly to Mercury.

<gai>Fir Bolg:<eng> one of the peoples who, according to ancient tradition, held Ireland. Apart from occupation before the Deluge, Ireland was invaded by (1) Partholon and his sons; (2) Neimheadh and his sons; (3) the Fir Bolg; (4) the Tuatha Dé Danann; (5) the sons of Milidh. In historic times there were invasions by the Norsemen about 800 A.D., and by the Normans, or rather by men from England and Wales, led by Norman knights.


Page 171.

<gai>Psaltair na Teamhrach:<eng> the Psalter of Tara, ascribed to Cormac mac Airt, and alluded to by Cuan O’Lochain, circ. 1000. On his authority it may be taken that in his time a book existed about the prerogatives of Tara and the provincial kings, so old that he, no doubt following tradition, was not afraid to ascribe it to Cormac mac Airt, who lived in the third century. So Professor Douglas Hyde, Literary Hist. of Ireland, p. 264.

<gai>cion firinne .i. meas firinne.<eng>


Page 172.

<gai>i n-allod .i. ’san aimsir i n-allod .i. ’san àm o shean.

cruinnchliatha caorthainn:<eng> rowan was used because it was a defence or prophylactic against the evil spirits, who, when summoned, had to be guarded against. The operators go <gai>i muinighin a ngeas,<eng> trusting to the power of their spells, but they lie on rowan hurdles as an additional precaution.

<gai>an togharmach san chiorcaill:<eng> the invoker of the spirits draws a circle round about him, and takes care to stand inside it. The demons whom he invokes cannot cross the circle.

The statement made by Keating in the first paragraph of this extract (pp. 171, 172) cannot be accepted. There is no real proof that the Irish Druids (1) had

[TD 241]

altars, (2) offered sacrifices thereon. Keating’s account is coloured by classical accounts of the Gaulish Druids and their practices. The Irish Druids were (1) diviners, (2) workers of magic or medicine men. They, and they alone, knew the proper and safe methods of dealing with evil spirits. They used their “science” for the benefit of the tribe. Each tribe had a druid, who was the king’s chief adviser. They used it also for the benefit, or the injury, of individuals. In both cases, their services were not without reward. The position of the Druids, both with respect to the tribe and to the individual, was taken over by the Church. The king’s chief adviser became not a druid, but a cleric. Demons were routed, not by spell and incantation, but by the sign of the cross. <gai>Is e mo drúi Críst,<eng> Christ is my Druid, a saying attributed to Colum Cille, accurately reflects the change. There was naturally bitter enmity between the Church and the Druids.


Page 173.

<gai>Lochlannaich:<eng> the period of Norse influence in Ireland is from 795 A.D., when the Norsemen plundered Lambay Island near Dublin, till 1014, when their power was broken by the Irish under Brian Boroimhe at the battle of Clontarf. Clontarf is now part of Dublin City.

<gai>gu cumair .i. gu h-aithghearr.

do bheireadh fear an tigh ris .i. bheireadh e fear an tighe leis.

i ngeall ré n-a bhuannacht .i. an urras a chairtealan.

athchaithte .i. air an caitheamh as;<eng> ath- intensive.

<gai>gan neart, etc., .i. gun chomas foghlum a theagasg no eaglaisean a thadhall.

náid:<eng> for <gai>ionáid,<eng> used here instead of <gai>ioná,<eng> “than,” “nor,” before a nominative plural.

<gai>is ar t-iarmhar da gcléir do mhair .i. is uiread de an cléir agus a mhair.<eng>

[TD 242]

Page 174.

<gai>do troisceadh leo air:<eng> fasting was resorted to in ancient Ireland as a means of forcing a debtor to pay. The creditor fasted at the door of the debtor. The debtor meanwhile was obliged to fast also, and to keep indoors as long as his creditor kept up the fast. This proceeding, strange to our views, was and is well known in the East. In Christian times this pagan practice, as well as others, was adapted to Christian usage.

<gai>Brian mac Cinnéididh:<eng> otherwise Brian Boroimhe, Brian of the cow-tribute, b. at Kincora 941, killed at Clontarf 1014; the Irish Wallace. His brother was king of Munster. After many years of fighting against the Danes and Norsemen, Brian deposed Maelsechlain, king of Ireland, in 1002, and was acknowledged king in his stead. After the decisive battle of Clontarf, Brian was attacked in his tent by a fleeing Norseman called Broder. Brian drew his sword and smote off both Broder’s legs with one blow, but the latter in falling cleft Brian’s head with his axe.

<gai>tug luach leabhair:<eng> Brian’s enlightened educational policy was in accordance with the great Irish tradition of encouragement of learning. In the sixth, seventh, and eighth centuries the schools of Ireland were numerous and famous. They were frequented by hosts of students from Britain, and even from the Continent. Bede, for instance, writing circ. 700 of the results of the yellow plague of 664, says:—“This pestilence prevailed no less disastrously in Ireland. Many of the nobility and of the lower ranks of the English nation were there at the time, who, in the days of the Bishops Finan and Colman, forsaking their native island, retired thither, either for the sake of sacred studies or of a more ascetic life. … The Scots [i.e., the Irish] willingly received them all, and took care to supply them with daily food without cost, as also to furnish them with books for their studies and teaching free of charge.”—Eccl. Hist. III., 27.

<gai>is e Brian fós tug sloinnte:<eng> it is not clear in what sense this is meant. The Irish tribes and septs were certainly differentiated long before Brian’s time.
