<gai>ag féchain a neithe … :<eng> “when we see the things printed in the press, how smartly and how quickly each one of them, notwithstanding its size, is completed thereby.”

<gai>Tuathaibh Dé Dhanond:<eng> “the tribes of the goddess Danau,” reputed ancient settlers of Ireland; v. note on p. 170, <gai>Fir Bolg.<eng>

Bishop Carswell’s attitude to the Gaelic heroic tales is typical of the Church of the Reformation. The pre-Reformation attitude is illustrated by the work of James MacGregor, Dean of Lismore, d. 1551, who was a great collector of the material despised by Bishop Carswell. It is, indeed, most likely that it is the Dean of Lismore, possibly also Donald Munro, High Dean of the Isles, that the Bishop has specially in view.


Page 180.

<gai>ar naineolas an drong so,<eng> etc.,: “our ignorance, we Gael of Alba, as I have mentioned already.”

<gai>as am briathruibh: as<eng> = out of, from; cf. <gai>tha e ag obair as a léinidh,<eng> he is working in his shirt sleeves.

<gai>ní rachaind féin a seilbh,<eng> etc.: “I would not take upon me to do the work”; a, prep. = in = Sc. G. <gai>an.

is mó iná mhacfaind:<eng> “greater than my means”; <gai>mo acfuinn,<eng> our <gai>acuinn,<eng> gear.

<gai>imarcaidh no easbhuidh:<eng> “excess or defect.”

<gai>acht mura,<eng> etc.: “unless indeed there is excess or defect therein according to the standard of diction or propriety laid down for Gaelic by the poets.”


Page 181.

<gai>ní ghébha mé cumairce na buidhne-sin:<eng> “I will not cry those folk mercy.”

<gai>madh áil le Dia .i. ma’s áil le Dia gu’n dèan so maith no tairbhe.<eng>

The text of this charter is now largely illegible; a fac-simile appears in Clan Donald, I. The lands granted consist of the present farms of Cornabus and Kintraw, and those adjacent to them on the

[TD 245]

south and west. The name “Wgasgog” has gone. <gai>Fercos Mac Betha,<eng> probably the writer of the charter, may have belonged to the famous family of physicians of that name, traditionally said to have come in the <gai>sochraid nemhghnáthach<eng> of p. 146.


Page 182.

<gai>Mac Domhnaill:<eng> Donald, Lord of the Isles, 1386-1423 circ.; v. note on p. 147.

<gai>én mhairg dég go leth: go<eng> formerly <gai>co,<eng> with; <gai>go leith<eng> = with a half. A merkland was about 35 acres.

<gai>air chunnrag(h) agus air choinghioll:<eng> “on covenant and on condition.”

<gai>da mharg agus da fhichit marg:<eng> this is at the rate of 10½ merks per cow, or 12s sterling. In the 14th century 9s was a good price for a mart; 12s seems high.

<gai>go deiriogh an bheatha .i. gu deireadh an t-saoghail.

do sheasamh agus do chongbháil:<eng> “to defend and maintain.”


Page 184.

<gai>Colum Cille:<eng> b. circ. 521 at Gartan, Donegal; d. 597 in Iona, to which he had gone in 563.

<gai>Drostan:<eng> his date is uncertain, and he is thought to have been rather prior to Columba and independent of him.

The legend of the founding of the monastery of Deer was written in the 11th century, and recounts tradition which is not necessarily accurate.

<gai>dattác .i. do atach;<eng> cf. <gai>ag gér atach,<eng> p. 177.


Page 186.

<gai>Malcoloum mac Cinatha:<eng> King Malcolm II., 1005-1034.

<gai>Malcolum mac Moilbrigte:<eng> d. 1029; called in Irish Annals <gai>rí Alban.

Malsnecte mac Luloig:<eng> d. 1085; called in the Irish Annals <gai>rí Moreb,<eng> king of Moray. His father, Lulach, was killed 1058.

The share <gai>(cuid)<eng> of king, mormaer, and toiseach consisted of dues in service, in maintenance, and in

[TD 246]

kind, to which each of these high officials were entitled from the land. The granting of the share means that the dues would thereafter be paid to the monastery.

Lands “in freedom from mormaer and from toiseach” would be free from all dues and exactions, except those due to the king.

Under the feudal system the <gai>Mormaer<eng> became Earl, and the <gai>Tòiseach<eng> became Thane.


Page 188.

<gai>in ocmad bliadin rígi Dabid:<eng> i.e., 1131-1132.

testibus istis: “these being the witnesses.” The Gaelic is a memorandum of a Latin charter. Nectan was the first bishop of Aberdeen; the see was founded about 1125.

<gai>Domongart ferléginn:<eng> the <gai>fer-léighenn<eng> was the head of the school which was taught in the monastery or in connection with it.

<gai>clann Canan:<eng> this and clann Morgainn, p. 190, are among the first clans met with in Scottish history. Of Clan Canan nothing more is known. Clan Morgan was long the designation of the Mackays of the Reay country, and it is probable, though perhaps not demonstrable, that there was a close connection between them and the Aberdeenshire Morgans.

Observe carefully to whom the grants are made in each case. The formula varies, and the prominence of Peter in III is an indication of strong Roman Catholic influence. All grants to churches, and all dedications, are to God in the first instance, though this is not always expressed.


Page 192.

dirationauerunt: supported by arguments made good by proof.

Gregory was bishop of Dunkeld, circ. 1135 to 1169.

Andrew was bishop of Caithness from about 1150.

[TD 247]

APPENDIX A.

In modern Gaelic the particle a may be—
A. the possessive pronoun 3 sg. mas. or fem.
B. the degraded form of do.
C. the degraded form of de.
D. the first letter of an (preposition).


A. Possessive pronoun:

(1) With ordinary possessive meaning and backward or retrospective reference. This use is so common that it needs little illustration.

<gai>Chuir e a làimh air a sròin,<eng> he put his hand on her nose (p. 55).

<gai>Cha robh h-uile ni g’ a riar,<eng> everything was not to her mind (p. 55).

<gai>Bha e ’n a chleachdadh,<eng> it was in its custom—i.e., it was a custom (p. 112).

<gai>Tha e dol air [a] ais,<eng> he is going back.

<gai>Tha e dol air [a] aghaidh,<eng> he is going on.

<gai>Tha i dol air a h-ais,<eng> she is going back.


(2) As object or “objective genitive” depending on a verbal noun, and with backward or retrospective reference:—

<gai>Dh’iarr e oirre a leantainn,<eng> lit. he asked of her his following—i.e., he asked her to follow him (p. 55).

<gai>A’ strì ri a pògadh,<eng> trying to kiss her (p. 55).

<gai>Tha an leac r’ a faicinn,<eng> lit. the slab is for its seeing—i.e., the slab is to be seen.

<gai>Tha [a] fhios agam,<eng> lit. I have its knowledge—i.e., I know.

<gai>Guidid Cuchulaind dia mathair a lécud dochum na macraide,<eng> lit. Cuchullin asks of his mother his letting to the lads—i.e., Cuchullin begs his mother to let him go to the lads—(Old Irish).


(3) As object or “objective genitive” depending on a verbal noun, and with forward or prospective reference:—

[TD 248]

<gai>Cha b’ urrainn e a ràdh gu’n do thuit a chrannchur,<eng> lit. he was not an authority (for) its saying that, etc.—i.e., he could not say that his lot had fallen (p. 66).

<gai>Gu mínáirech ag a rádha nach dlighfedis na prindsadha,<eng> lit. shamelessly at its saying that, etc.—i.e., saying shamelessly that princes ought not (p. 178).

<gai>ag a rádh ris gan cádhus do thabhairt,<eng> lit. at its saying to him without reverence to give—i.e., bidding him not to give reverence—(Keating).

<gai>faodar a chreidsinn nach robh Lot sona,<eng> lit. its believing is possible that Lot was not happy—i.e., it may be believed that Lot was not happy (p. 68).

<gai>Biodh [a] fhios agad gu bheil sin mar sin,<eng> lit. let its knowledge be with you, etc.—i.e., know that that is so.

<gai>Biodh fios agad gur b’ e Raghnall,<eng> know that it was Raghnall. Here, if the text is right, the poss. pron. is omitted altogether. In the previous example its presence is indicated by aspiration.

<gai>atá a fhios agamsa go ndénadh,<eng> lit. I have its knowledge that he would do—i.e., I know that he would do.


(4) With prospective reference in certain phrases:—

<gai>Cha chuala mi a bheag de na bha e ag ràdh.<eng>
I heard nothing of what he was saying.

<gai>Cha’n fhaca mi riamh a leithid so de dhuine.<eng>
I never saw the like of this man.


B. The degraded form of do, the verbal particle.

(1) With force of relative pronoun as subject or object; a very common use.

<gai>Na daoine a chunnaic mi féin.
Na daoine do chonnaic mi féin (p. 152).<eng>
The men whom I saw myself.

[TD 249]

<gai>A’ cheud arm a chuireadh air chois.
An chét arm do cuiredh ar chois (p. 152).<eng>
The first army that was set on foot.

<gai>Gach duine a dh’ eireadh leo.
Gach duine do eirghed leó (p. 153).<eng>
Every man who would rise with them.


(2) After various adverbial expressions of time, etc.

<gai>Am feadh a mhair an cogadh.
Am fedh do mhair an cogadh (p. 154).<eng>
While the war lasted.

<gai>Cha’n fhada a chaidh iad.
Ní fada do chúadar (p. 156).<eng>
They had not gone far.

<gai>Mar a ghabh e tighearnas.
Mar do ghabh tighernas (p. 1488).<eng>
How he took lordship.

<gai>Is ’n a aghaidh a bha e.
Is na aghaidh do bhí sé (p. 152).

Is motha a cheannsaich e.
Is mó do cheannsaich, etc. (p. 145).<eng>
He subdued more.


(3) The degraded form of do, the preposition, expressing purpose:—

<gai>Thainig Clann an Aba chuca a chomhnadh.
Táinic clann an Aba chuca do chongnamh (p. 155).<eng>
The MacNabs came to them to help.

<gai>Chuireadh fir a bhràighe air leth a dheanamh creachadh.
Do cuiredh fir an bhraghad ar leth do dhénamh creachadh (p. 155).<eng>
The up-country men were selected to make a plundering expedition.

[TD 250]

<gai>An uair a dh’ éirich Cormac a thaisteal an fhuinn.
Dar eirigh Cormac do thaisteal an fhuinn (p. 159).<eng>
When Cormac rose to traverse the land.

<gai>Tha e dol a Pheirt; a dh’ Eirinn; a choimhead.<eng>
He is going to Perth; to Ireland; to see.


(4) The degraded form of do with verbal noun, in the peculiar construction illustrated below. Here do with verbal noun is practically equivalent to a true infinitive, and the combination is preceded by an object.

<gai>Cha ghnàth leis na buill a mholadh.
Ni ghnàth na buill do mholadh (p. 150).<eng>
He is not wont to praise the members.

<gai>Is furusda dhuit fios [a] fhaighinn.
Is furusda dhuit fios d’ fhaghail (p. 152).<eng>
You can easily ascertain.

<gai>An déidh coimhcheangal a dhèanamh.
Taréis coimhchengal do dhénamh (p. 152).<eng>
After making a covenant.

<gai>Eadar an cath sin a thabhairt agus cath eile a bhrisdeadh.
Eder an cath sin do thabhairt agus cath eile do bhrisdeadh (p. 154).<eng>
Between fighting that battle and winning another.

<gai>A chum cath a thabhairt dhoibh.
Do chum catha do thabhairt doibh (p. 155).<eng>
In order to give them battle.

<gai>Thubhairt e ris an leanabh a chleith.
Adubhairt ris an leanbh do cheilt (p. 161).<eng>
He bade him conceal the child.

[TD 251]

<gai>Thachair e dhomhsa mac an righ a mharbhadh.
Tarla dhomhsa mac an ríogh do mharbhadh (p. 161).<eng>
It has befallen me to slay the king’s son.

<gai?An fhirinn a chluinntinn.
An fhirinde do chluisdin (p. 180).<eng>
To hear the truth.

<gai>An t-saothair-sa a dhèanamh
An tsaothair-se do dhénamh (p. 180).<eng>
To take this trouble.

<gai>Cha’n ’eil e ag guidhe dioghaltas a thabhairt orra.
ní guid dígail du thabairt foraib (Milan Glosses, 42a4).<eng>
He does not pray that vengeance be taken on them.


(5) In certain phrases:—

<gai>a réir<eng> for <gai>do réir,<eng> according to, lit. to will.

<gai>a ghnáth<eng> for <gai>do ghnáth,<eng> continually.

<gai>a thaobh<eng> for <gai>do thaobh,<eng> as regards.

<gai>a chum<eng> for <gai>do chum,<eng> towards, in order to.


C. Degraded form of de: colloquial, and not often found in good writers:—

<gai>Cha’n ’eil a bheag a dhaoine ann (for de dhaoine).<eng>
There are not any men.


D. (1) The first letter of a preposition an “from,” found in the adverbs (or adverbial expressions):—

<gai>a nall,<eng> from over, to this side; an and root seen in <gai>thall.

a nìos,<eng> from below; an and *ìos, down, seen in <gai>iosal, sios.

a nuas,<eng> from above; an and *uas, up, seen in <gai>uasal, suas.

a null,<eng> over, to beyond; for nunn, on analogy of <gai>a nall.<eng>

[TD 252]

(2) The first letter of the preposition an, “in,” “to,” found in the expressions:—

<gai>a bhàn,<eng> down, for a bhfàn, i.e. a nfàn, into slope, down.

<gai>a bhos,<eng> on this side, for a bhfos, i.e. a nfos, in station at rest.

<gai>a mach,<eng> outside (motion to), for an with accusative, “into,” and <gai>magh,<eng> a field.

<gai>a muigh,<eng> outside (rest), for an with dative, “in,” and <gai>maigh,<eng> dat. of <gai>magh.

a nis,<eng> now, Ir. anois, for an and <gai>fois,<eng> rest.

<gai>a steach,<eng> within (motion), for <gai>in-san-tech,<eng> into the house.

<gai>a stigh,<eng> within (rest), for <gai>in-san-tigh,<eng> in the house.


APPENDIX B.

ECLIPSIS.

1. Eclipsis is the modification of the initial consonant of a word, as the result of contact with a final n of a closely connected preceding word. This final n was carried on to the next word by a process comparable to the French liaison.


2. The words after which eclipsis occurs are classified for the present purpose as follows:—

(1) The prepositions in, in; gon, with; ren, before; iarn, afterwards; and the conjunctions gon, so that (same as preposition originally) dán, if.

(2) The numerals seacht, ocht, naoí, deich.

(3) The accusative singular and genitive plural of the article, and of any noun or adjective.

(4) The possessives arn, our; bhurn, your; an, their; the relative an (really the article).

(5) Some nouns originally neuter show eclipsis after the nominative singular. In the old language this was the case with all neuter nouns.

[TD 253]

3. If the second word begins with a vowel, the final n of the preceding word is written before the vowel, usually with a hyphen—

e.g., ar n-athar, our father.

If the second word begins with a consonant, its initial

b is written mb, pronounced m
c is written gc (cc), pronounced g
d is written nd, pronounced n
f is written bfh, pronounced v
g is written ng, pronounced ng
p is written bp, pronounced b
t is written dt (tt), pronounced d

Note:—
C = Carswell.
Ch = Macdonald Charter.
D = Book of Deer.
K = Keating.
M = MacVurich.


4. Examples—(1) Prepositions and Conjunctions:—

in:—i n-éalódh (K), in elopement; a gcló (C), in print; i gginn (D), i gcionn (K), a ccionn (M), at head, to meet; i dtaisgidh (K), in keeping; i ngaois (K), in wisdom; a ccruth (M), in shape; a ccomhdhail (M), in meeting; a Ccill Moilridh (K), in Kilmaree; a bhfogus (M), near; i bhfios (K), in knowledge; i bpéin (K), under penalty.

gon:—co n-a nasgad (D), with gift of it; go tteobhraidh (Ch.), that he give; go bhfacaidh (K), so that he saw; go ttugadh (M), that he would give; go gceilfeadh (K), that he would conceal; go mbeireadh (K), that he would bring.

ren:—re n-a mhnaoi (K), to his wife; re n-a athair, with his father (M); re n-abarthar (M), to whom is said.

iarn:—ar n-ég d’ athair (M), after death to his father; on his father’s death; ar n-a chlos sin (K), on hearing that; ar gcúl (C), behind;

[TD 254]

ar dtús, at first (K); ar ngluasacht (M), after moving.

dán:—dá bfaicind (C), if I were to see; dá gcriothnuighdís a airm (K), if his weapons shook; dá mbeirthi air (K), if he was caught.


(2) Numerals:—seacht n-airghe (K), seven herds; seacht bhfichid (K), seven score; seacht gcatha (K), seven battalions; ocht n-aoidhche (M), eight nights.


(3) acc. sing. and gen. pl.:—chaillech ndubh (M), of nuns; na dtarbh n-iodhbarta (K), of the sacrificed bulls; air son na mbó cceadna (Ch.), for the aforesaid cows; iar n-ére na glérec (D), after refusing the clerics; d’attac na glérec (D), to supplicate the clerics; na bhfiaghain (Ch.), of the witnesses.


(4) ar a ginn (D), on their head, before them; ri n-a dtoruibh, with their fruits; ar n-aineolas (C), our ignorance; a bhfian bhoth, of their hunting booths.


(5) lá n-aon, one day.

Additional examples should be collected from p. 143 onwards.


APPENDIX C.

THE IRISH REGULAR VERB.

The following paradigm of a regular verb is taken from O’Donovan’s Irish Grammar. The stem of molaim is broad. Slender stems, like ceilim, I conceal, will, of course, have their final consonant flanked with slender vowels throughout. Thus, molamaoid, we praise; ceilimíd, we conceal; molfad, I shall praise; ceilfead, I shall conceal. Other slight differences need not concern us here.

[TD 255]

IRREGULAR VERBS.

Those parts only are given which occur in the text. It will be noted that certain verbs possess independent forms and dependent forms of the same tense. The question of the origin of these forms could be discussed

[TD 256]

intelligibly only with reference to the older stages of the language, and is, therefore, omitted here. It may be said, however, that in most cases the double form arises from a difference of accentuation in compound verbs. Thus in O. Ir. do and berim accented on the second part give do.biur, I give (the dot is put before the accented syllable); accented on the preposition they give .tabur, I give; do and gníu give do.gníu, and .denim, I do. Here do.biur and do.gníu are the independent or absolute forms; .tabur and .dénim are dependent forms. The dependent form of the verb is used in the imperative mood, and after ní, not; an and nach, interrogative; go, that; cá, where; muna, unless; dá, if; and the relative governed by a preposition.

In the case of Scottish Gaelic the stress accent is always on the first syllable of a compound word.

NOTE 1.—In the 3 sing. preterite there appears in addition to the uninflected form (usually with do), a form in -is e.g. gabhais (in MacVurich gabhas) and do ghabh; fiafruighis and do fhiafruigh; cuiris and do chuir. Other examples are: targes 144; tairges, diultais, ghluaisis 153; geallais, aontuighis 160; glacais, tillis, leigis, screadais 161.

Note 2.—Similarly beside the usual 3 pl. preterite in -adar there appears a form in -sat (-seat), the old 3 pl. ending of s-preterite verbs. Thus, tugsad 144; do leansad 157; doronsat 184; doratsat 186, 188. Though Keating uses this form freely, no example happens to occur in the extracts. Carswell does not use it, nor does he use the 3 sing. pret. in -is.

<gai>Gleann Ruaidh,<eng> 101, Glenroy; <gai>Ruaidh<eng> is the name of the river. In Pictish usage, the river gives its name (1) to the lake (if any) from which it issues, (2) to the glen or strath through which it flows, (3) to the spot where it enters the sea or another river. In Irish and purely Gaelic usage the river does not necessarily do any of these things

<gai>Graftol,<eng> 183

<gai>Grianaig,<eng> 3, Greenock; prob. “sunny spot”: cf. <gai>grianan.<eng> There is a Greenock near Callander, in G. Grianaig

<gai>Grianan Deardail,<eng> 42, Deirdre’s sunny seat; so tradition; but <gai>deardail<eng> also means storm, din, and as the <gai>grianan<eng> is usually the sharp top of a hill, which the sun strikes first and leaves last, the meaning may be “sunny place of storm”

<gai>Grìminis,<eng> 138, 141: probably O.N. Grímu-nes, from the woman’s name Gríma, or the common noun gríma, hood, mask, figurehead of a ship


<gai>Haisgeir,<eng> 20; O.N. haf-sker, deep-sea skerry

<gai>Hèisger,<eng> 20; older <gai>Helsker;<eng> fr. O.N. hellu-sker, flat-stone skerry. Hel(jar)sker, Hell skerry, is perhaps also possible. The influence of l or ll remains in the lingering, drawn out pronunciation of the first syllable, as in soi(ll)se, boi(ll)sgeadh, Goi(ll)sbidh, Golspie

<gai>Hogmór,<eng> 150: Howmore; O.N. haugr, a burial mound, with mór, big, to distinguish it from Howbeg. O.N. au regularly becomes ō in Gaelic

<gai>Helain,<eng> 190, Ellon

<gai>Hoster,<eng> 141 (for Hòsta, o nasal), ? O.N. Högna-stadhr, Hogni’s stead

[TD 270]

<gai>I, Iona,<eng> 26: “Ioua insula,” Adam. (Ioua adj..); Hy, Híí, Bede, c. 730; E.Ir. n. Hí; I Columbae Cille combusta est; genitive Ia, Ie; dative Híí, Hí. Meaning unknown. “Iona” has arisen from a mistaken reading of “Ioua” by Hector Boece. So “Hebrides” is a mistake for “Hebudae.” These are “ghost-names”

<gai>Ile (ī),<eng> Islay; Ilea insula Adamnan); meaning obscure

<gai>Inbhir-faragaig,<eng> 42, Inverfarigaig; “Mouth of Farigaig,” the stream through <gai>Srathfharagaig,<eng> Stratherrick; <gai>far<eng> and <gai>gág,<eng> “above cleft,” with reference to the deep and narrow defile near the mouth of the river

<gai>Inbher Feothfaramh<eng> (rightly, <gai>Inbhir Pheofharan<eng>), 146, Dingwall “Peffer mouth”

<gai>Inbher Lòchaigh,<eng> 155: Inverlochy, “Mouth of Lochy.” Adamnan (Vit. Col., 110, 134) mentions a loch Stagnum Loch-diae, which Columba passed while he was journeying trans Britanniæ dorsum.
On p. 178 he mentions as in Lochaber fluvius qui Latine dici potest Nigra Dea. As lòch is O. Gael. for “black,” it is clear that Nigra Dea is to be equated with Lōch-dia. The stagnum is Loch Lòchaidh. There are several other streams of the same name, e.g. at Killin, Loch Tay; Tyndrum; Banffshire

<gai>Inbher-nis,<eng> 150, Inverness; “Ness-mouth”; the first mention of the Ness is in Adamnan’s Vit. Col.: “fluvium transire Nesam,” p. 170; “Et hoc coram Brudeo rege dicens, domum egressus regiam, ad Nesam venit fluvium,” p. 174; “ad lacum Nesae fluminis longum venit,” p. 176. Nesa was a river-goddess

<gai>Innis Cealtrach,<eng> 175; a holy island in L. Derg, Galway

<gai>Innse-gall,<eng> 36, the Hebrides; “isles of the strangers,” i.e. Norsemen

<gai>Innse Geala nan Ra-soluis,<eng> 30

<gai>h-Innse na,<eng> 9, the Indies; dat. pl. <gai>na h-Innsibh

h-Irt,<eng> 31, St Kilda; O.Ir. hirt .i. <gai>bás.<eng> St Kilda is a ghost-name. A well on the island, called Tobar Childa, from O.N. kelda, a well, was confused by map-makers of 17th century with the true name of the island


<gai>Ladharn,<eng> 145, Lorne; gen. <gai>Ladharna;<eng> see <gai>Comhal

Láirc Thurraid<eng> 156, pass of Turret at the head of Glen Roy. Turret is a stream name; root tur, dry, “shrinker”

<gai>Learga Riabhoige,<eng> 183, “the brindled slopes”

<gai>Leòdhas,<eng> 35, etc., Lewis; Ljódhús and Ljódhus in the Norse Sagas; E.Ir. Leódús; O.N. ljódhahús, “lay house,” “song house”; possibly “people’s house”

<gai>Litcu,<eng> 149, Linlithgow: usually in Gaelic <gai>Gleann-iucha.<eng> One of the seven wonders of Scotland was <gai>tobraichean Ghlinn-iucha,<eng> the wells of Linlithgow

<gai>Lìte,<eng> 153, Leith; formerly Inverleith, Leith being the name of the river; cf. Inner-leithen, Peebles

<gai>Loch-abar,<eng> 99, 155, Lochaber; the district between the headwaters of Roy and Spean on the north and Loch Leven on the south, with Loch Linnhe on the west and Badenoch to

[TD 271]

the east. “In regione quae Stagno Aporum est contermina,” Adamnan, Vit. Col., 164. “In regione quae Stagni litoribus Aporici est contermina,” ib., 177. Derived by Dr MacBain from <gai>eabar,<eng> mud, puddle; Ir. abar, marshy land. Tradition makes the original Lochaber a small loch in the <gai>Mòine Mhór,<eng> near the mouth of the river Lochy, now drained

<gai>Loch Alann,<eng> 153, Loch Aline; “Beautiful Loch” ?

<gai>Loch-buidhe,<eng> 113, 151, in Mull

<gai>Loch Carrann,<gai> 41, Lochcarron; *Carsonā; root kars-, rough, seen in carr, carraig, carrach; Eng. harsh. (Proto-Celtic -rs- becomes in Gaelic -rr-)

<gai>Loch Dubhthaich,<eng> 40, Loch Duich; “St Duthac’s Loch”

<gai>Loch Eiseord,<eng> 153, in Skye; Norse; ord represents fjördhr, inlet, firth: first part uncertain

<gai>Loch Eite,<eng> 42, Loch Etive; Mid. Ir. Eitchi, Eitche; the name properly belongs to the river Etive, and appears to be connected with éitigh, fierce; O. Ir. étig, foul. The v of Etive represents, as often, Gaelic gh between vowels, e.g. <gai>Ceann-ruighe,<eng> Kin-rive. Observe that Eitchi, Eitche, are genitive of a nom. Eitig; cf. n. sétig, g. séitche

<gai>Loch Laoimunn,<eng> 118: Loch Lomond; in Welsh Llumonwy

<gai>Loch Lòchaidh,<eng> 150, Loch Lochy (see <gai>Inbher-lòchaidh<eng>)

<gai>Loch Tairbeirt,<eng> 117: Loch of the Portage; <gai>tar,<eng> across, and <gai>berim,<eng> I bring.

<gai>Loch Tatha,<eng> 40, Loch Tay; Tacitus ad Taum (otherwise, Tavum, Tavam); O.Ir. Tói (g. and d.); E.Ir. “im Thai .i. ainm srotha in Albain”: about Tay, that is, the name of a river in Alba; from *tavos, tava, quiet, silent

<gai>Lochlann,<eng> 20, Scandinavia; g. <gai>Lochlainn

Loinneachan,<eng> 15

<gai>Lurchari,<eng> 190


<gai>Machaire,<eng> 183, “the plain”

<gai>Mana,<eng> 144, Isle of Man; g. <gai>Manann;<eng> d. <gai>Manainn

Maol Chinn-tìre,<eng> 36, Mull of Cantyre; <gai>maol,<eng> a blunt headland; <gai>ceann-tìre,<eng> “land’s end”; “caput regionis,” Adam.; <gai>sruthair na Máile Chind Tíri,<eng> the current off the Mull of Cantyre, Bk. of Leinster

<gai>Marr,<eng> 99, 188; district in Aberdeenshire, part of the Dee valley. Its early form would, according to rule, have been *Mars-, with which Dr Whitley Stokes compared the Latin tribe Marsi, and the Marsigni on the north border of Bohemia

<gai>Meghrath (Meaghrath),<eng> 152, Reay; a compound of which the second part is ràth, earthwork, fort

<gai>Monadh Dubh,<eng> 43, Black Mount

<gai>a’ Mhorbharn,<eng> 144, Morvern; perhaps mor and bearn, “sea-gap”; gen. <gai>na Morbhairne

Mide,<eng> 159, Meath; gen. <gai>na Midhe

Míoghari,<eng> 153, Mingarry; Norse; meaning uncertain

<gai>Múideort,<eng> 151, Moidart; Norse; -ort represents O.N. fjördr, a firth, sea loch; the first part is uncertain

<gai>Muile,<eng> 149, Mull; Ptolemy (circ. 125 A.D.) Malaios; Adamnan, Vit. Col., Malea insula. Meaning unknown

<gai>Mumha,<eng> 146, Munster; gen. <gai>na Mumhan

[TD 272]

Neibheis,<eng> 157 (Nibheis), R. Nevis; see <gai>Beinn Nibheis


Obar-dheadhain,<eng> 8, 154, Aberdeen; “Mouth of Don”; obar, from od-ber-, out-put; the Don rises in <gai>Coire Dheadhain

Obuir-thairbh,<eng> 147, Abertarff; “Bull’s Outlet”; <gai>tarbh<eng> is the name of the river

<gai>Oilen Eorsaigh,<eng> 147; E. of Ulva

<gai>Oilen Fionnlagain,<eng> 147, Isle of Finlagan, “white hollow,” in Islay

<gai>Oilen t-Suibhne,<eng> 147, Isle Sween, in Loch Sween

<gai>Orti,<eng> 186


<gai>am Parbh,<eng> 36, Cape Wrath; O.N. hvarf, from hverfa, to turn; in Lewis called an Carbh, with change of p to c, as in <gai>Càisg<eng> = pascha; <gai>cailleach,<eng> nun, from <gai>caille,<eng> a veil, Lat. pallium, etc.

<gai>Peart,<eng> Perth, 40, 154; Welsh perth, a brake, thicket; British or Pictish, not Gaelic; so Pert, Logie-Pert, Lar-bert

<gai>Pet ipair,<eng> 188

<gai>Pett in Mulenn,<eng> 186; “Baile a’ Mhuilinn”; pett is very common on the east from Forth to Rogart, and is practically equivalent to G. <gai>baile

<gai>Pinnging Mhór,<eng> 157, Pennymore, in Argyll

<gai>Pórainn,<eng> 34, Porin, “Pasture place”


<gai>Réilig Odhrain,<eng> 112, 145: St Odhran’s burial place, in Iona. Odhran, says tradition, was the first buried therein.

<gai>Rocabarraidh,<eng> 30, 31

<gai>an Roinn-eorpa,<eng> 36, Europe; “division of Europe; n. <gai>Eorap;<eng> g. <gai>h-Eorpa;<eng> d. <gai>Eoraip

Ros,<eng> 36, Ross; ros, from prosto-, means (1) promontory, (2) wood, in Scotland usually the former, e.g. <gai>an Ros Muileach,<eng> a long promontory in Mull; <gai>Coillerois,<eng> wood-point; <gai>Ros-cuibhne,<eng> Rosskeen, antler-point

<gai>an Ros Muileach,<eng> 111: the Ross of Mull

<gai>Rosabard,<eng> 186

<gai>Rudha Aird-na-Murchann,<eng> 30, Ardnamurchan Point; n. pl. <gai>Artda Muirchol;<eng> g. pl. <gai>Art Muirchol;<eng> d. pl. <gai>in Artdaib Muirchol,<eng> Adam., meaning “Heights of Muirchol.” Two possible suggestions are made for muirchol—(1) sea hazel (coll); (2) sea of Coll, the island. But see Cola. In the modern form as above -nn is a corruption of old -l

<gai>Rudha Robhanis,<eng> 38, the Butt of Lewis; O.N. rofanes, hole-cape, with reference to the Eye of the Butt

<gai>Ruathainn,<eng> 77, Ruthven, in Badenoch, and elsewhere; better <gai>Ruadhainn; cf. Gealainn,<eng> Glen Lyon and Fortingall; based on <gai>ruadh,<eng> red, and <gai>geal,<eng> white; the ending may be the locative of fonn, soil, which would account for v of Ruthven

<gai>Ruigh an Fhìdhleir,<eng> 100; “the fiddler’s reach”; <gai>ruighe<eng> means a slope reaching up to a hill, or, a cleared spot

<gai>an Ròimh,<eng> 136, Rome; gen. <gai>na Ròimhe

<gai>Seile,<eng> 144, R. Shiel; root of seile, saliva; cf. Seile of Glenshiel, Ross-shire; <gai>an Linne Sheileach,<eng> Loch Linnhe, so called, it is said, owing to the quantity of fresh water in it

<gai>Serusalaim,<eng> 145, Jerusalem

<gai>Sgeir na Baintighearna,<eng> 115: the lady’s skerry; a sgeir is usually covered at high water

<gai>Sion,<eng> 2

<gai>Sìthphort,<eng> 152, Seaforth; Norse; <gai>phort<eng> represents fjördhr, an inlet; first part perhaps sídhu-, from <gai>sídha,<eng> sloping country, a special use of sídha, side

<gai>Srath Chluaidh,<eng> 35, Strathclyde; “Srath of river Clyde”; Clōta, Tacitus, c. 80 A.D., and Ptol.; Petra Cloithe, “Rock of Clyde,” Dumbarton, Adam.; urbem Alcluith quod lingua eorum significat petram Cluith, Bede; Kaer Alcluth, O. Welsh for Dumbarton, E. Ir. Ail Clúade; root clou-, cleanse; Clōta is a goddess name like Nessa, etc.

<gai>Srath-chonuinn,<eng> 34, Strath-conon; “Strath of the Conon river”

<gai>Srath Mhathaisidh,<eng> 77, Strathmashie; in Badenoch; meaning uncertain, but compare <gai>Caolaisidh, Móraisidh, Breacaisidh, Cruaidhsidh,<eng> etc., in all which ’isidh is locative of <gai>innis,<eng> a pasture, meadow, haugh. This, therefore, may be <gai>maith<eng> and <gai>innse,<eng> good pasture; the opposite of <gai>Ath-aisidh,<eng> from <gai>ath-innse,<eng> worn out, poor pasture

<gai>Srath Oisein,<eng> 41; “Ossian’s Strath”; some miles S.E. of <gai>Ceann Locha Tréig,<eng> Inverness-shire

<gai>Srath Thatha,<eng> 40, Strath Tay; see Loch Tatha

<gai>Sròn nam Fiann,<eng> 40, “Point of the Fiann,” in Glendaruel

<gai>Staoiligearraidh,<eng> 139

<gai>Steòrnabhaigh,<eng> 22, Stornoway; O.N. Stjórna-vágr; probably “helm-bay”

Tailte,<eng> 172, Teltown, in Meath; old n. <gai>Tailtiu;<eng> gen. <gai>Tailtean.<eng> “Till recently Teltown was resorted to by the Meathmen for hurling, wrestling,” etc. Hogan: Onomasticon

<gai>Teamhair,<eng> 146, Tara; gen. <gai>(na) Teamhrach,<eng> dat. <gai>Teamhraigh;<eng> “a place of wide prospect”; from tem-, cut; thus, “a cut-off place,” “a hillock”

<gai>Teampall Odhran,<eng> 148, 149, in Iona

<gai>Tiobraid Arann,<eng> 158, Tipperary; “Well of Arú,” a goddess

<gai>Tir-iodh,<eng> 58, Tiree; also <gai>Tir-idhe,<eng> “Land of Corn”; from O.Ir. ith, g. etho, corn, seen in <gai>iodh-lann.<eng> In Adamnan

[TD 274]

it is “Ethica Terra”; he has also “Ethicum pelagus.” Its fertility is proverbial: <gai>Tir-idhe iosal an eòrna.<eng> “Qua est ordei magna copia”—Fordun. Adj. <gai>Tiristeach.<eng> Cf. Cornabus

<gai>Tìr-nam-beò,<eng> 20, the Land of the living; opposed to <gai>Tìr-fo-thuinn,<eng> Land under wave

<gai>Tìr nan Og,<eng> 30, 31, the Land of the Young; the happy otherworld, where there is no sickness or want or death; sometimes placed in an isle of the Western Sea, sometimes under waves. In the older writings it is <gai>Mag Mell,<eng> the pleasant plain; <gai>Mag már,<eng> the great plain; <gai>Tír Beó,<eng> land of the living; <gai>Tír n-aill,<eng> the other world; <gai>Tír Tairrngire,<eng> the land of promise

<gai>Tocamol,<eng> 183

[TD 279]

FACLAIR

Words which occur in Maceachen’s Gaelic-English Dictionary are, as a rule, not given in this glossary. Irish Gaelic verbs, here given in the 1st p. singular of the present indicative active, are explained by Scottish Gaelic verbs in the 2nd singular imperative. When the spelling in the text differs from the standard modern form, the latter follows it in parentheses.

A baile should keep four herds of cows of 75 each. A seisreach was as much as one plough could turn.—Joyce, Soc. Hist., I., 40

place,” which must therefore have developed in Gaelic. Cf. <gai>cluain,<eng> (1) meadow, (2) burying-ground (Armstrong)
